<td class="mbox-text">
<div class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. <p>In <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">radio</a> an <b>antenna</b> is the interface between <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio waves</a> propagating through space and electric currents moving in metal conductors, used with a <a href="/wiki/Transmitter" title="Transmitter">transmitter</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Receiver_(radio)" title="Receiver (radio)">receiver</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graf_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graf-1">[1]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Transmission_(telecommunications)" title="Transmission (telecommunications)">transmission</a>, a radio transmitter supplies an electric current to the antenna's terminals, and the antenna radiates the energy from the current as <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic waves</a> (radio waves). In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Receiver_(radio)" title="Receiver (radio)">reception</a>, an antenna intercepts some of the power of an electromagnetic wave in order to produce an electric current at its terminals, that is applied to a receiver to be <a href="/wiki/Amplifier" title="Amplifier">amplified</a>. Antennas are essential components of <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">radio</a> equipment, and are used in <a href="/wiki/Radio_broadcasting" title="Radio broadcasting">radio broadcasting</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broadcast_television" title="Broadcast television">broadcast television</a>, <a href="/wiki/Two-way_radio" title="Two-way radio">two-way radio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Communications_receiver" title="Communications receiver">communications receivers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cell_phone" title="Cell phone">cell phones</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satellite_communications" title="Satellite communications">satellite communications</a> and other devices.</p>
<p>An antenna is an array of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conductor_(material)" title="Conductor (material)">conductors</a> (<a href="/wiki/Driven_element" title="Driven element">elements</a>), electrically connected to the receiver or transmitter. During transmission, the oscillating current applied to the antenna by a transmitter creates an oscillating <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> around the antenna elements. These time-varying fields radiate energy away from the antenna into space as a moving <a href="/wiki/Transverse_wave" title="Transverse wave">transverse</a> electromagnetic field wave. Conversely, during reception, the oscillating electric and magnetic fields of an incoming radio wave exert force on the electrons in the antenna elements, causing them to move back and forth, creating oscillating currents in the antenna.</p>
<p>Antennas can be designed to transmit and receive radio waves in all horizontal directions equally (<a href="/wiki/Omnidirectional_antenna" title="Omnidirectional antenna">omnidirectional antennas</a>), or preferentially in a particular direction (<a href="/wiki/Directional_antenna" title="Directional antenna">directional</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_gain_antenna" title="High gain antenna">high gain</a> antennas). An antenna may include <a href="/wiki/Passive_radiator" title="Passive radiator">parasitic elements</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_antenna" title="Parabolic antenna">parabolic reflectors</a> or <a href="/wiki/Horn_antenna" title="Horn antenna">horns</a>, which serve to direct the radio waves into a beam or other desired <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pattern" title="Radiation pattern">radiation pattern</a>.</p>
<p>The first antennas were built in 1888 by German physicist <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Hertz" title="Heinrich Hertz">Heinrich Hertz</a> in his pioneering experiments to prove the existence of electromagnetic waves predicted by the theory of <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a>. Hertz placed <a href="/wiki/Dipole_antenna" title="Dipole antenna">dipole antennas</a> at the focal point of <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_reflector" title="Parabolic reflector">parabolic reflectors</a> for both transmitting and receiving. He published his work in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annalen_der_Physik_und_Chemie" title="Annalen der Physik und Chemie">Annalen der Physik und Chemie</a></i> (vol. 36, 1889).</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dipole_xmting_antenna_animation_4_408x318x150ms.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="318" data-file-width="408" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Dipole_xmting_antenna_animation_4_408x318x150ms.gif/310px-Dipole_xmting_antenna_animation_4_408x318x150ms.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a6/Dipole_xmting_antenna_animation_4_408x318x150ms.gif 1.5x" width="310"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dipole_xmting_antenna_animation_4_408x318x150ms.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Animation of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half-wave_dipole" title="Half-wave dipole">half-wave dipole</a> antenna transmitting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_waves" title="Radio waves">radio waves</a>, showing the <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a> lines. The antenna in the center is two vertical metal rods, with an alternating current applied at its center from a radio transmitter <i>(not shown)</i>. The voltage charges the two sides of the antenna alternately positive <i><span style="color:red;">(+)</span></i> and negative <i><span style="color:blue;">(−)</span></i>. Loops of electric field <i>(black lines)</i> leave the antenna and travel away at the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>; these are the radio waves.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dipole_receiving_antenna_animation_6_800x394x150ms.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="394" data-file-width="800" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Dipole_receiving_antenna_animation_6_800x394x150ms.gif/330px-Dipole_receiving_antenna_animation_6_800x394x150ms.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Dipole_receiving_antenna_animation_6_800x394x150ms.gif/495px-Dipole_receiving_antenna_animation_6_800x394x150ms.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Dipole_receiving_antenna_animation_6_800x394x150ms.gif/660px-Dipole_receiving_antenna_animation_6_800x394x150ms.gif 2x" width="330"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dipole_receiving_antenna_animation_6_800x394x150ms.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Animated diagram of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half-wave_dipole" title="Half-wave dipole">half-wave dipole</a> antenna receiving energy from a radio wave. The antenna consists of two metal rods connected to a receiver <i>R</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a> <i>(<span style="color:green;">E, green arrows</span>)</i> of the incoming wave pushes the <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> in the rods back and forth, charging the ends alternately positive <i><span style="color:red;">(+)</span></i> and negative <i><span style="color:blue;">(−)</span></i>. Since the length of the antenna is one half the <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> of the wave, the oscillating field induces <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave" title="Standing wave">standing waves</a> of voltage <i>(<span style="color:red;">V, represented by red band</span>)</i> and current in the rods. The oscillating currents <i>(black arrows)</i> flow down the transmission line and through the receiver (represented by the resistance <i>R</i>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:77px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AntennaSymbol.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="565" data-file-width="175" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/AntennaSymbol.png/75px-AntennaSymbol.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/AntennaSymbol.png/113px-AntennaSymbol.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/AntennaSymbol.png/150px-AntennaSymbol.png 2x" width="75"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AntennaSymbol.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Electronic symbol for an antenna</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The words <i>antenna</i> and <i>aerial</i> are used interchangeably. Occasionally the term "aerial" is used to mean a wire antenna. The origin of the word <i>antenna</i> relative to wireless apparatus is attributed to Italian radio pioneer <a href="/wiki/Guglielmo_Marconi" title="Guglielmo Marconi">Guglielmo Marconi</a>. In the summer of 1895, Marconi began testing his wireless system outdoors on his father's estate near <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a> and soon began to experiment with long wire "aerials".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> In Italian a tent pole is known as <i>l'antenna centrale,</i> and the pole with the wire was simply called <i>l'antenna.</i> Until then wireless radiating transmitting and receiving elements were known simply as aerials or terminals. Because of his prominence, Marconi's use of the word <i>antenna</i> spread among wireless researchers, and later to the general public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slyusar1_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-slyusar1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slyusar2_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-slyusar2-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slyusar3_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-slyusar3-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Antenna</i> may refer broadly to an entire assembly including support structure, enclosure (if any), etc. in addition to the actual functional components. Especially at microwave frequencies, a receiving antenna may include not only the actual electrical antenna but an integrated preamplifier or <a href="/wiki/Frequency_mixer" title="Frequency mixer">mixer</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text">
<div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. </td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Atacama_Large_Millimeter_submillimeter_Array_(ALMA)_by_night_under_the_Magellanic_Clouds.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/The_Atacama_Large_Millimeter_submillimeter_Array_%28ALMA%29_by_night_under_the_Magellanic_Clouds.jpg/220px-The_Atacama_Large_Millimeter_submillimeter_Array_%28ALMA%29_by_night_under_the_Magellanic_Clouds.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/The_Atacama_Large_Millimeter_submillimeter_Array_%28ALMA%29_by_night_under_the_Magellanic_Clouds.jpg/330px-The_Atacama_Large_Millimeter_submillimeter_Array_%28ALMA%29_by_night_under_the_Magellanic_Clouds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/The_Atacama_Large_Millimeter_submillimeter_Array_%28ALMA%29_by_night_under_the_Magellanic_Clouds.jpg/440px-The_Atacama_Large_Millimeter_submillimeter_Array_%28ALMA%29_by_night_under_the_Magellanic_Clouds.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Atacama_Large_Millimeter_submillimeter_Array_(ALMA)_by_night_under_the_Magellanic_Clouds.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Antennas of the <a href="/wiki/Atacama_Large_Millimeter_Array" title="Atacama Large Millimeter Array">Atacama Large Millimeter submillimeter Array</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Antennas are required by any radio receiver or transmitter to couple its electrical connection to the electromagnetic field. <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">Radio</a> waves are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_waves" title="Electromagnetic waves">electromagnetic waves</a> which carry signals through the air (or through space) at the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> with almost no <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Absorption (electromagnetic radiation)">transmission loss</a>. Radio transmitters and receivers are used to convey signals in broadcast (audio) radio, <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mobile_telephones" title="Mobile telephones">mobile telephones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wi-Fi" title="Wi-Fi">Wi-Fi</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WLAN" title="WLAN">WLAN</a>) data networks, and <a href="/wiki/Remote_control" title="Remote control">remote control</a> devices among many others. Radio waves are also used directly for measurements in <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPS" title="GPS">GPS</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Radio_astronomy" title="Radio astronomy">radio astronomy</a>. Transmitters and receivers require antennas, although these are sometimes hidden (such as the antenna inside an AM radio or inside a laptop computer equipped with Wi-Fi).</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Car_radio_antenna_extended_portrait.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2304" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Car_radio_antenna_extended_portrait.jpeg/150px-Car_radio_antenna_extended_portrait.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Car_radio_antenna_extended_portrait.jpeg/225px-Car_radio_antenna_extended_portrait.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Car_radio_antenna_extended_portrait.jpeg/300px-Car_radio_antenna_extended_portrait.jpeg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Car_radio_antenna_extended_portrait.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Whip_antenna" title="Whip antenna">Whip antenna</a> on car, common example of an omnidirectional antenna</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Antennas can be classified as <a href="/wiki/Omnidirectional_antenna" title="Omnidirectional antenna">omnidirectional</a>, radiating energy approximately equally in all directions, or <a href="/wiki/Directional_antenna" title="Directional antenna">Directional</a>, where energy radiates more along one direction than others. (Antennas are reciprocal, so the same effect occurs for reception of radio waves.) A completely uniform omnidirectional antenna is not physically possible. Many important antenna types have a uniform radiation pattern in the horizontal plane, but send little energy upward or downward. A "directional" antenna usually is intended to maximize its coupling to the electromagnetic field in the direction of the other station.</p>
<p>One example of omnidirectional antennas is the very common <i>vertical antenna</i> or <a href="/wiki/Whip_antenna" title="Whip antenna">whip antenna</a> consisting of a metal rod. A <a href="/wiki/Dipole_antenna" title="Dipole antenna">dipole antenna</a> is similar but consists of two such conductors extending in opposite directions. Dipoles are typically oriented horizontally in which case they are weakly directional: signals are reasonably well radiated toward or received from all directions with the exception of the direction along the conductor itself; this region is called the antenna blind cone or null.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Half_%E2%80%93_Wave_Dipole.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Half_%E2%80%93_Wave_Dipole.jpg/150px-Half_%E2%80%93_Wave_Dipole.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Half_%E2%80%93_Wave_Dipole.jpg/225px-Half_%E2%80%93_Wave_Dipole.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Half_%E2%80%93_Wave_Dipole.jpg/300px-Half_%E2%80%93_Wave_Dipole.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Half_%E2%80%93_Wave_Dipole.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Half-wave <a href="/wiki/Dipole_antenna" title="Dipole antenna">dipole antenna</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Both the vertical and dipole antennas are simple in construction and relatively inexpensive. The dipole antenna, which is the basis for most antenna designs, is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balanced" title="Balanced">balanced</a> component, with equal but opposite voltages and currents applied at its two terminals through a <a href="/wiki/Balanced_line" title="Balanced line">balanced transmission line</a> (or to a coaxial transmission line through a so-called <a href="/wiki/Balun" title="Balun">balun</a>). The vertical antenna, on the other hand, is a <i><a href="/wiki/Monopole_antenna" title="Monopole antenna">monopole</a></i> antenna. It is typically connected to the inner conductor of a <a href="/wiki/Coaxial_cable" title="Coaxial cable">coaxial transmission line</a> (or a matching network); the shield of the transmission line is connected to <a href="/wiki/Ground_(electricity)" title="Ground (electricity)">ground</a>. In this way, the ground (or any large conductive surface) plays the role of the second conductor of a dipole, thereby forming a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circuit_theory#Open_circuit_vs._closed_circuit" title="Circuit theory">complete circuit</a>. Since monopole antennas rely on a conductive ground, a so-called <a href="/wiki/Ground_(electricity)" title="Ground (electricity)">grounding</a> structure may be employed to provide a better ground contact to the earth or which itself acts as a <a href="/wiki/Ground_plane" title="Ground plane">ground plane</a> to perform that function regardless of (or in absence of) an actual contact with the earth.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Felder_um_Dipol.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="600" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Felder_um_Dipol.jpg/150px-Felder_um_Dipol.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Felder_um_Dipol.jpg/225px-Felder_um_Dipol.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Felder_um_Dipol.jpg/300px-Felder_um_Dipol.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Felder_um_Dipol.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diagram of the <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric fields</a> <i><span style="color:blue;">(blue)</span></i> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a> <i><span style="color:red;">(red)</span></i> radiated by a <a href="/wiki/Dipole_antenna" title="Dipole antenna">dipole antenna</a> <i>(black rods)</i> during transmission.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Antennas more complex than the dipole or vertical designs are usually intended to increase the directivity and consequently the gain of the antenna. This can be accomplished in many different ways leading to a plethora of antenna designs. The vast majority of designs are fed with a balanced line (unlike a monopole antenna) and are based on the dipole antenna with additional components (or <i>elements</i>) which increase its directionality. Antenna "gain" in this instance describes the concentration of radiated power into a particular solid angle of space, as opposed to the spherically uniform radiation of the ideal radiator. The increased power in the desired direction is at the expense of that in the undesired directions. Power is conserved, and there is no net power increase over that delivered from the power source (the transmitter.)</p>
<p>For instance, a <a href="/wiki/Phased_array" title="Phased array">phased array</a> consists of two or more simple antennas which are connected together through an electrical network. This often involves a number of parallel dipole antennas with a certain spacing. Depending on the relative <a href="/wiki/Phase_(waves)" title="Phase (waves)">phase</a> introduced by the network, the same combination of dipole antennas can operate as a "broadside array" (directional normal to a line connecting the elements) or as an "end-fire array" (directional along the line connecting the elements). Antenna arrays may employ any basic (omnidirectional or weakly directional) antenna type, such as dipole, loop or slot antennas. These elements are often identical.</p>
<p>However a <a href="/wiki/Log-periodic_antenna" title="Log-periodic antenna">log-periodic dipole array</a> consists of a number of dipole elements of <i>different</i> lengths in order to obtain a somewhat directional antenna having an extremely wide bandwidth: these are frequently used for television reception in fringe areas. The dipole antennas composing it are all considered "active elements" since they are all electrically connected together (and to the transmission line). On the other hand, a superficially similar dipole array, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yagi-Uda_Antenna" title="Yagi-Uda Antenna">Yagi-Uda Antenna</a> (or simply "Yagi"), has only one dipole element with an electrical connection; the other so-called <a href="/wiki/Passive_radiator" title="Passive radiator">parasitic elements</a> interact with the electromagnetic field in order to realize a fairly directional antenna but one which is limited to a rather narrow bandwidth. The Yagi antenna has similar looking parasitic dipole elements but which act differently due to their somewhat different lengths. There may be a number of so-called "directors" in front of the active element in the direction of propagation, and usually a single (but possibly more) "reflector" on the opposite side of the active element.</p>
<p>Greater directionality can be obtained using beam-forming techniques such as a <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_reflector" title="Parabolic reflector">parabolic reflector</a> or a horn. Since high directivity in an antenna depends on it being large compared to the wavelength, narrow beams of this type are more easily achieved at UHF and microwave frequencies.</p>
<p>At low frequencies (such as AM broadcast), arrays of vertical towers are used to achieve directionality<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and they will occupy large areas of land. For reception, a long <a href="/wiki/Beverage_antenna" title="Beverage antenna">Beverage antenna</a> can have significant directivity. For non directional portable use, a short vertical antenna or small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_loop_antenna" title="Magnetic loop antenna">loop antenna</a> works well, with the main design challenge being that of <a href="/wiki/Impedance_matching" title="Impedance matching">impedance matching</a>. With a vertical antenna a <i>loading coil</i> at the base of the antenna may be employed to cancel the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_reactance" title="Electrical reactance">reactive component of impedance</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_loop_antenna" title="Magnetic loop antenna">small loop antennas</a> are tuned with parallel capacitors for this purpose.</p>
<p>An antenna lead-in is the <a href="/wiki/Transmission_line" title="Transmission line">transmission line</a> (or <i><a href="/wiki/Feed_line" title="Feed line">feed line</a></i>) which connects the antenna to a transmitter or receiver. The <i><a href="/wiki/Antenna_feed" title="Antenna feed">antenna feed</a></i> may refer to all components connecting the antenna to the transmitter or receiver, such as an <a href="/wiki/Impedance_matching" title="Impedance matching">impedance matching</a> network in addition to the transmission line. In a so-called aperture antenna, such as a horn or parabolic dish, the "feed" may also refer to a basic antenna inside the entire system (normally at the focus of the parabolic dish or at the throat of a horn) which could be considered the one active element in that antenna system. A microwave antenna may also be fed directly from a <a href="/wiki/Waveguide" title="Waveguide">waveguide</a> in place of a (conductive) <a href="/wiki/Transmission_line" title="Transmission line">transmission line</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:6_sector_site_in_CDMA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="502" data-file-width="339" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/6_sector_site_in_CDMA.jpg/150px-6_sector_site_in_CDMA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/6_sector_site_in_CDMA.jpg/225px-6_sector_site_in_CDMA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/6_sector_site_in_CDMA.jpg/300px-6_sector_site_in_CDMA.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:6_sector_site_in_CDMA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cell_phone" title="Cell phone">Cell phone</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cellular_base_station" title="Cellular base station">base station</a> antennas</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An antenna <a href="/wiki/Counterpoise_(ground_system)" title="Counterpoise (ground system)">counterpoise</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ground_plane" title="Ground plane">ground plane</a> is a structure of conductive material which improves or substitutes for the ground. It may be connected to or insulated from the natural ground. In a monopole antenna, this aids in the function of the natural ground, particularly where variations (or limitations) of the characteristics of the natural ground interfere with its proper function. Such a structure is normally connected to the return connection of an unbalanced transmission line such as the shield of a <a href="/wiki/Coaxial_cable" title="Coaxial cable">coaxial cable</a>.</p>
<p>An electromagnetic wave <i>refractor</i> in some aperture antennas is a component which due to its shape and position functions to selectively delay or advance portions of the electromagnetic wavefront passing through it. The refractor alters the spatial characteristics of the wave on one side relative to the other side. It can, for instance, bring the wave to a focus or alter the wave front in other ways, generally in order to maximize the directivity of the antenna system. This is the radio equivalent of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_lens" title="Optical lens">optical lens</a>.</p>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Antenna_tuner" title="Antenna tuner">antenna coupling network</a> is a passive network (generally a combination of inductive and capacitive circuit elements) used for <a href="/wiki/Impedance_matching" title="Impedance matching">impedance matching</a> in between the antenna and the transmitter or receiver. This may be used to improve the <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave_ratio" title="Standing wave ratio">standing wave ratio</a> in order to minimize losses in the transmission line and to present the transmitter or receiver with a standard resistive impedance that it expects to see for optimum operation.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reciprocity">Reciprocity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Reciprocity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>It is a fundamental property of antennas that the electrical characteristics of an antenna described in the next section, such as <a href="/wiki/Antenna_gain" title="Antenna gain">gain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pattern" title="Radiation pattern">radiation pattern</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrical_impedance" title="Electrical impedance">impedance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bandwidth_(signal_processing)" title="Bandwidth (signal processing)">bandwidth</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resonant_frequency" title="Resonant frequency">resonant frequency</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a>, are the same whether the antenna is <a href="/wiki/Transmitter" title="Transmitter">transmitting</a> or <a href="/wiki/Radio_receiver" title="Radio receiver">receiving</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lonngren_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lonngren-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stutzman_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stutzman-9">[9]</a></sup> For example, the "<i>receiving pattern</i>" (sensitivity as a function of direction) of an antenna when used for reception is identical to the <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pattern" title="Radiation pattern">radiation pattern</a> of the antenna when it is <i>driven</i> and functions as a radiator. This is a consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Reciprocity_(electromagnetism)" title="Reciprocity (electromagnetism)">reciprocity theorem</a> of electromagnetics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stutzman_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stutzman-9">[9]</a></sup> Therefore, in discussions of antenna properties no distinction is usually made between receiving and transmitting terminology, and the antenna can be viewed as either transmitting or receiving, whichever is more convenient.</p>
<p>A necessary condition for the aforementioned reciprocity property is that the materials in the antenna and transmission medium are <a href="/wiki/Linear_function" title="Linear function">linear</a> and reciprocal. <i>Reciprocal</i> (or <i>bilateral</i>) means that the material has the same response to an electric current or magnetic field in one direction, as it has to the field or current in the opposite direction. Most materials used in antennas meet these conditions, but some microwave antennas use high-tech components such as <a href="/wiki/Isolator_(microwave)" title="Isolator (microwave)">isolators</a> and <a href="/wiki/Circulator" title="Circulator">circulators</a>, made of nonreciprocal materials such as <a href="/wiki/Ferrite_(iron)" title="Ferrite (iron)">ferrite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lonngren_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lonngren-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stutzman_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stutzman-9">[9]</a></sup> These can be used to give the antenna a different behavior on receiving than it has on transmitting,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lonngren_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lonngren-8">[8]</a></sup> which can be useful in applications like <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text">
<div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(January 2014)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Antenna_measurement#Antenna_parameters" title="Antenna measurement">Antenna measurement § Antenna parameters</a></div>
<p>Antennas are characterized by a number of performance measures which a user would be concerned with in selecting or designing an antenna for a particular application. Chief among these relate to the directional characteristics (as depicted in the antenna's <i><a href="/wiki/Radiation_pattern" title="Radiation pattern">radiation pattern</a></i>) and the resulting <i><a href="/wiki/Antenna_gain" title="Antenna gain">gain</a></i>. Even in omnidirectional (or weakly directional) antennas, the gain can often be increased by concentrating more of its power in the horizontal directions, sacrificing power radiated toward the sky and ground. The antenna's <a href="/wiki/Antenna_gain" title="Antenna gain">power gain</a> (or simply "gain") also takes into account the antenna's efficiency, and is often the primary figure of merit.</p>
<p>Resonant antennas are expected to be used around a particular <i><a href="/wiki/Resonance" title="Resonance">resonant frequency</a></i>; an antenna must therefore be built or ordered to match the frequency range of the intended application. A particular antenna design will present a particular feedpoint <a href="/wiki/Electrical_impedance" title="Electrical impedance">impedance</a>. While this may affect the choice of an antenna, an antenna's impedance can also be adapted to the desired impedance level of a system using a <a href="/wiki/Impedance_matching" title="Impedance matching">matching network</a> while maintaining the other characteristics (except for a possible loss of efficiency).</p>
<p>Although these parameters can be <a href="/wiki/Antenna_measurement" title="Antenna measurement">measured</a> in principle, such measurements are difficult and require very specialized equipment. Beyond tuning a transmitting antenna using an <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave_ratio" title="Standing wave ratio">SWR</a> meter, the typical user will depend on theoretical predictions based on the antenna design or on claims of a vendor.</p>
<p>An antenna transmits and receives radio waves with a particular <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a> which can be reoriented by tilting the axis of the antenna in many (but not all) cases. The physical size of an antenna is often a practical issue, particularly at lower frequencies (longer wavelengths). Highly directional antennas need to be significantly larger than the wavelength. Resonant antennas usually use a linear conductor (or <i>element</i>), or pair of such elements, each of which is about a quarter of the wavelength in length (an odd multiple of quarter wavelengths will also be resonant). Antennas that are required to be small compared to the wavelength sacrifice efficiency and cannot be very directional. At higher frequencies (UHF, microwaves) trading off performance to obtain a smaller physical size is usually not required.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resonant_antennas">Resonant antennas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Resonant antennas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dipole_antenna_standing_waves_animation_461x217x150ms.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="217" data-file-width="461" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Dipole_antenna_standing_waves_animation_461x217x150ms.gif/330px-Dipole_antenna_standing_waves_animation_461x217x150ms.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/10/Dipole_antenna_standing_waves_animation_461x217x150ms.gif 1.5x" width="330"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dipole_antenna_standing_waves_animation_461x217x150ms.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Standing_wave" title="Standing wave">Standing waves</a> on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half_wave_dipole" title="Half wave dipole">half wave dipole</a> driven at its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resonant_frequency" title="Resonant frequency">resonant frequency</a>. The waves are shown graphically by bars of color (<span style="color:red;">red for voltage, <i>V</i></span> and <span style="color:blue;">blue for current, <i>I</i></span>) whose width is proportional to the amplitude of the quantity at that point on the antenna.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The majority of antenna designs are based on the <i>resonance</i> principle. This relies on the behaviour of moving electrons, which reflect off surfaces where the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dielectric_constant" title="Dielectric constant">dielectric constant</a> changes, in a fashion similar to the way light reflects when optical properties change. In these designs, the reflective surface is created by the end of a conductor, normally a thin metal wire or rod, which in the simplest case has a <i>feed point</i> at one end where it is connected to a <a href="/wiki/Transmission_line" title="Transmission line">transmission line</a>. The conductor, or <i>element</i>, is aligned with the electrical field of the desired signal, normally meaning it is perpendicular to the line from the antenna to the source (or receiver in the case of a broadcast antenna).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The radio signal's electrical component induces a voltage in the conductor. This causes an electrical current to begin flowing in the direction of the signal's instantaneous field. When the resulting current reaches the end of the conductor, it reflects, which is equivalent to a 180 degree change in phase. If the conductor is <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span> of a wavelength long, current from the feed point will undergo 90 degree phase change by the time it reaches the end of the conductor, reflect through 180 degrees, and then another 90 degrees as it travels back. That means it has undergone a total 360 degree phase change, returning it to the original signal. The current in the element thus adds to the current being created from the source at that instant. This process creates a <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave" title="Standing wave">standing wave</a> in the conductor, with the maximum current at the feed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHall199125_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHall199125-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ordinary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half-wave_dipole" title="Half-wave dipole">half-wave dipole</a> is probably the most widely used antenna design. This consists of two <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span>-wavelength elements arranged end-to-end, and lying along essentially the same axis (or <i>collinear</i>), each feeding one side of a two-conductor transmission wire. The physical arrangement of the two elements places them 180 degrees out of phase, which means that at any given instant one of the elements is driving current into the transmission line while the other is pulling it out. The <a href="/wiki/Monopole_antenna" title="Monopole antenna">monopole antenna</a> is essentially one half of the half-wave dipole, a single <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span>-wavelength element with the other side connected to <a href="/wiki/Ground_(electricity)" title="Ground (electricity)">ground</a> or an equivalent <a href="/wiki/Ground_plane" title="Ground plane">ground plane</a> (or <i><a href="/wiki/Counterpoise_(ground_system)" title="Counterpoise (ground system)">counterpoise</a></i>). Monopoles, which are one-half the size of a dipole, are common for long-wavelength radio signals where a dipole would be impractically large. Another common design is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folded_dipole" title="Folded dipole">folded dipole</a>, which is essentially two dipoles placed side-by-side and connected at their ends to make a single one-wavelength antenna.</p>
<p>The standing wave forms with this desired pattern at the design frequency, <i>f<sub>0</sub></i>, and antennas are normally designed to be this size. However, feeding that element with <i>3f<sub>0</sub></i> (whose wavelength is <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>3</sub></span> that of <i>f<sub>0</sub></i>) will also lead to a standing wave pattern. Thus, an antenna element is <i>also</i> resonant when its length is <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>3</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span> of a wavelength. This is true for all odd multiples of <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span> wavelength. This allows some flexibility of design in terms of antenna lengths and feed points. Antennas used in such a fashion are known to be <i>harmonically operated</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHall199131-32_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHall199131-32-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_and_voltage_distribution">Current and voltage distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Current and voltage distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The quarter-wave elements imitate a <a href="/wiki/LC_circuit" title="LC circuit">series-resonant</a> electrical element due to the standing wave present along the conductor. At the resonant frequency, the standing wave has a current peak and voltage node (minimum) at the feed. In electrical terms, this means the element has minimum <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reactance_(electronics)" title="Reactance (electronics)">reactance</a>, generating the maximum current for minimum voltage. This is the ideal situation, because it produces the maximum output for the minimum input, producing the highest possible efficiency. Contrary to an ideal (lossless) series-resonant circuit, a finite resistance remains (corresponding to the relatively small voltage at the feed-point) due to the antenna's <a href="/wiki/Radiation_resistance" title="Radiation resistance">radiation resistance</a> as well as any actual electrical losses.</p>
<p>Recall that a current will reflect when there are changes in the electrical properties of the material. In order to efficiently send the signal into the transmission line, it is important that the transmission line has the same <a href="/wiki/Electrical_impedance" title="Electrical impedance">impedance</a> as the elements, otherwise some of the signal will be reflected back into the antenna. This leads to the concept of <a href="/wiki/Impedance_matching" title="Impedance matching">impedance matching</a>, the design of the overall system of antenna and transmission line so the impedance is as close as possible, thereby reducing these losses. Impedance matching between antennas and transmission lines is commonly handled through the use of a <a href="/wiki/Balun" title="Balun">balun</a>, although other solutions are also used in certain roles. An important measure of this basic concept is the <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave_ratio" title="Standing wave ratio">standing wave ratio</a>, which measures the magnitude of the reflected signal.</p>
<p>Consider a half-wave dipole designed to work with signals 1 m wavelength, meaning the antenna would be approximately 50 cm across. If the element has a length-to-diameter ratio of 1000, it will have an inherent resistance of about 63 ohms. Using the appropriate transmission wire or balun, we match that resistance to ensure minimum signal loss. Feeding that antenna with a current of 1 ampere will require 63 volts of RF, and the antenna will radiate 63 watts (ignoring losses) of radio frequency power. Now consider the case when the antenna is fed a signal with a wavelength of 1.25 m; in this case the reflected current would arrive at the feed out-of-phase with the signal, causing the net current to drop while the voltage remains the same. Electrically this appears to be a very high impedance. The antenna and transmission line no longer have the same impedance, and the signal will be reflected back into the antenna, reducing output. This could be addressed by changing the matching system between the antenna and transmission line, but that solution only works well at the new design frequency.</p>
<p>The end result is that the resonant antenna will efficiently feed a signal into the transmission line only when the source signal's frequency is close to that of the design frequency of the antenna, or one of the resonant multiples. This makes resonant antenna designs inherently narrowband, and they are most commonly used with a single target signal. They are particularly common on <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> systems, where the same antenna is used for both broadcast and reception, or for radio and television <i>broadcasts</i>, where the antenna is working with a single frequency. They are less commonly used for reception where multiple channels are present, in which case additional modifications are used to increase the bandwidth, or entirely different antenna designs are used.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrically_short_antennas">Electrically short antennas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Electrically short antennas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>It is possible to use simple impedance matching concepts to allow the use of monopole or dipole antennas substantially shorter than the ¼ or ½ wavelength, respectively, at which they are resonant. As these antennas are made shorter (for a given frequency) their impedance becomes dominated by a series capacitive (negative) reactance; by adding a series inductance with the opposite (positive) reactance – a so-called <a href="/wiki/Loading_coil#Radio_antenna" title="Loading coil">loading coil</a> – the antenna's reactance may be cancelled leaving only a pure resistance. Sometimes the resulting (lower) electrical resonant frequency of such a system (antenna plus matching network) is described using the concept of <i><a href="/wiki/Electrical_length" title="Electrical length">electrical length</a></i>, so an antenna used at a lower frequency than its resonant frequency is called an <i>electrically short antenna</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEEE_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEEE-13">[13]</a></sup>.</p>
<p>For example, at 30 MHz (10 m wavelength) a true resonant ¼ wavelength monopole would be almost 2.5 meters long, and using an antenna only 1.5 meters tall would require the addition of a loading coil. Then it may be said that the coil has lengthened the antenna to achieve an electrical length of 2.5 meters. However, the resulting resistive impedance achieved will be quite a bit lower than that of a true ¼ wave (resonant) monopole, often requiring further impedance matching (a transformer) to the desired transmission line. For ever shorter antennas (requiring greater "electrical lengthening") the radiation resistance plummets (approximately according to the square of the antenna length), so that the mismatch due to a net reactance away from the electrical resonance worsens. Or one could as well say that the equivalent resonant circuit of the antenna system has a higher <a href="/wiki/Q_factor" title="Q factor">Q factor</a> and thus a reduced bandwidth<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEEE_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-IEEE-13">[13]</a></sup>, which can even become inadequate for the transmitted signal's spectrum. <a href="/wiki/Copper_loss" title="Copper loss">Resistive losses</a> due to the loading coil, relative to the decreased radiation resistance, entail a reduced <a href="#Efficiency">electrical efficiency</a>, which can be of great concern for a transmitting antenna, but bandwidth is the major factor<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (June 2016)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Antenna_(radio)#Dubious" title="Talk:Antenna (radio)">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> that sets the size of antennas at 1 MHz and lower frequencies.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrays_and_reflectors">Arrays and reflectors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Arrays and reflectors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antenna.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="250" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Antenna.jpg/220px-Antenna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/Antenna.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antenna.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rooftop television <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yagi-Uda_antenna" title="Yagi-Uda antenna">Yagi-Uda</a> antennas like these are widely used at <a href="/wiki/Very_high_frequency" title="Very high frequency">VHF</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ultra_high_frequency" title="Ultra high frequency">UHF</a> frequencies.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The amount of signal received from a distant transmission source is essentially geometric in nature due to the <a href="/wiki/Inverse-square_law" title="Inverse-square law">inverse-square law</a>, and this leads to the concept of <i><a href="/wiki/Antenna_aperture" title="Antenna aperture">effective area</a></i>. This measures the performance of an antenna by comparing the amount of <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">power</a> it generates to the amount of power in the original signal, measured in terms of the signal's power density in Watts per square metre. A half-wave dipole has an effective area of 0.13 <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \lambda }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\lambda " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b43d0ea3c9c025af1be9128e62a18fa74bedda2a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.366ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span><sup>2</sup>. If more performance is needed, one cannot simply make the antenna larger. Although this would intercept more energy from the signal, due to the considerations above, it would decrease the output significantly due to it moving away from the resonant length. In roles where higher performance is needed, designers often use multiple elements combined together.</p>
<p>Returning to the basic concept of current flows in a conductor, consider what happens if a half-wave dipole is not connected to a feed point, but instead shorted out. Electrically this forms a single <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span>-wavelength element. But the overall current pattern is the same; the current will be zero at the two ends, and reach a maximum in the center. Thus signals near the design frequency will continue to create a standing wave pattern. Any varying electrical current, like the standing wave in the element, will radiate a signal. In this case, aside from resistive losses in the element, the rebroadcast signal will be significantly similar to the original signal in both magnitude and shape. If this element is placed so its signal reaches the main dipole in-phase, it will reinforce the original signal, and increase the current in the dipole. Elements used in this way are known as <i>passive elements</i>.</p>
<p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yagi-Uda_antenna" title="Yagi-Uda antenna">Yagi-Uda</a> array uses passive elements to greatly increase gain. It is built along a support boom that is pointed toward the signal, and thus sees no induced signal and does not contribute to the antenna's operation. The end closer to the source is referred to as the front. Near the rear is a single active element, typically a half-wave dipole or folded dipole. Passive elements are arranged in front (<i>directors</i>) and behind (<i>reflectors</i>) the active element along the boom. The Yagi has the inherent quality that it becomes increasingly directional, and thus has higher gain, as the number of elements increases. However, this also makes it increasingly sensitive to changes in frequency; if the signal frequency changes, not only does the active element receive less energy directly, but all of the passive elements adding to that signal also decrease their output as well and their signals no longer reach the active element in-phase.</p>
<p>It is also possible to use multiple active elements and combine them together with transmission lines to produce a similar system where the phases add up to reinforce the output. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antenna_array_(electromagnetic)" title="Antenna array (electromagnetic)">antenna array</a> and very similar <a href="/wiki/Reflective_array_antenna" title="Reflective array antenna">reflective array antenna</a> consist of multiple elements, often half-wave dipoles, spaced out on a plane and wired together with transmission lines with specific phase lengths to produce a single in-phase signal at the output. The <a href="/wiki/Log-periodic_antenna" title="Log-periodic antenna">log-periodic antenna</a> is a more complex design that uses multiple in-line elements similar in appearance to the Yagi-Uda but using transmission lines between the elements to produce the output.</p>
<p>Reflection of the original signal also occurs when it hits an extended conductive surface, in a fashion similar to a mirror. This effect can also be used to increase signal through the use of a <i><a href="/wiki/Reflector_(antenna)" title="Reflector (antenna)">reflector</a></i>, normally placed behind the active element and spaced so the reflected signal reaches the element in-phase. Generally the reflector will remain highly reflective even if it is not solid; gaps less than <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>10</sub></span> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \lambda }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\lambda " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b43d0ea3c9c025af1be9128e62a18fa74bedda2a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.366ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> generally have little effect on the outcome. For this reason, reflectors often take the form of wire meshes or rows of passive elements, which makes them lighter and less subject to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wind_Engineering" title="Wind Engineering">wind-load effects</a>, of particular importance when mounted at higher elevations with respect to the surrounding structures. The <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_reflector" title="Parabolic reflector">parabolic reflector</a> is perhaps the best known example of a reflector-based antenna, which has an effective area far greater than the active element alone.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bandwidth">Bandwidth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Bandwidth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although a resonant antenna has a purely resistive feed-point impedance at a particular frequency, many (if not most) applications require using an antenna over a range of frequencies. The frequency range or <i><a href="/wiki/Bandwidth_(signal_processing)" title="Bandwidth (signal processing)">bandwidth</a></i> over which an antenna functions well can be very wide (as in a log-periodic antenna) or narrow (in a resonant antenna); outside this range the antenna impedance becomes a poor match to the transmission line and transmitter (or receiver). Also in the case of the Yagi-Uda and other end-fire arrays, use of the antenna well away from its design frequency affects its <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pattern" title="Radiation pattern">radiation pattern</a>, reducing its directive gain; the usable bandwidth is then limited regardless of impedance matching.</p>
<p>Except for the latter concern, the resonant frequency of an antenna system can always be altered by adjusting a suitable matching network. This is most efficiently accomplished using a matching network at the site of the antenna, since simply adjusting a matching network at the transmitter (or receiver) would leave the transmission line with a poor <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave_ratio" title="Standing wave ratio">standing wave ratio</a>.</p>
<p>Instead, it is often desired to have an antenna whose impedance does not vary so greatly over a certain bandwidth. It turns out that the amount of reactance seen at the terminals of a resonant antenna when the frequency is shifted, say, by 5%, depends very much on the diameter of the conductor used. A long thin wire used as a half-wave dipole (or quarter wave monopole) will have a reactance significantly greater than the resistive impedance it has at resonance, leading to a poor match and generally unacceptable performance. Making the element using a tube of a diameter perhaps 1/50 of its length, however, results in a reactance at this altered frequency which is not so great, and a much less serious mismatch and effect on the antenna's net performance. Thus rather thick tubes are often used for the elements; these also have reduced <a href="/wiki/Copper_loss" title="Copper loss">parasitic resistance</a> (loss).</p>
<p>Rather than just using a thick tube, there are similar techniques used to the same effect such as replacing thin wire elements with <i>cages</i> to simulate a thicker element. This widens the bandwidth of the resonance. On the other hand, it is desired for <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio" title="Amateur radio">amateur radio</a> antennas to operate at several bands which are widely separated from each other (but not in between). This can often be accomplished simply by connecting elements resonant at those different frequencies in parallel. Most of the transmitter's power will flow into the resonant element while the others present a high (reactive) impedance, thus drawing little current from the same voltage. Another popular solution uses so-called <i>traps</i> consisting of parallel resonant circuits which are strategically placed in breaks along each antenna element. When used at one particular frequency band the trap presents a very high impedance (parallel resonance) effectively truncating the element at that length, making it a proper resonant antenna. At a lower frequency the trap allows the full length of the element to be employed, albeit with a shifted resonant frequency due to the inclusion of the trap's net reactance at that lower frequency.</p>
<p>The bandwidth characteristics of a resonant antenna element can be characterized according to its <a href="/wiki/Q_factor" title="Q factor">Q</a>, just as one uses to characterize the sharpness of an <a href="/wiki/LC_circuit" title="LC circuit">L-C resonant circuit</a>. A common mistake is to assume that there is an advantage in an antenna having a high <a href="/wiki/Q_factor" title="Q factor">Q</a> (the so-called "quality factor"). In the context of electronic circuitry a low Q generally signifies greater loss (due to unwanted resistance) in a resonant <a href="/wiki/LC_circuit" title="LC circuit">L-C circuit</a>, and poorer receiver <a href="/wiki/Selectivity_(electronic)" title="Selectivity (electronic)">selectivity</a>. However this understanding does not apply to resonant antennas where the resistance involved is the <a href="/wiki/Radiation_resistance" title="Radiation resistance">radiation resistance</a>, a desired quantity which removes energy from the resonant element in order to radiate it (the purpose of an antenna, after all!). The Q of an L-C-R circuit is defined as the ratio of the inductor's (or capacitor's) reactance to the resistance, so for a certain <a href="/wiki/Radiation_resistance" title="Radiation resistance">radiation resistance</a> (the radiation resistance at resonance does not vary greatly with diameter) the greater reactance off-resonance causes the poorer bandwidth of an antenna employing a very thin conductor. The Q of such a narrowband antenna can be as high as 15. On the other hand, the reactance at the same off-resonant frequency of one using thick elements is much less, consequently resulting in a Q as low as 5. These two antennas may perform equivalently at the resonant frequency, but the second antenna will perform over a bandwidth 3 times as wide as the antenna consisting of a thin conductor.</p>
<p>Antennas for use over much broader frequency ranges are achieved using further techniques. Adjustment of a matching network can, in principle, allow for any antenna to be matched at any frequency. Thus the <a href="/wiki/Loop_antenna#Small_loop_receiving_antennas" title="Loop antenna">loop antenna</a> built into most AM broadcast (medium wave) receivers has a very narrow bandwidth, but is tuned using a parallel capacitance which is adjusted according to the receiver tuning. On the other hand, log-periodic antennas are <i>not</i> resonant at any frequency but can be built to attain similar characteristics (including feedpoint impedance) over any frequency range. These are therefore commonly used (in the form of directional <a href="/wiki/Log-periodic_antenna" title="Log-periodic antenna">log-periodic dipole arrays</a>) as television antennas.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gain">Gain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Gain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Antenna_gain" title="Antenna gain">Antenna gain</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Antenna_gain" title="Antenna gain">Gain</a> is a parameter which measures the degree of <a href="/wiki/Directivity" title="Directivity">directivity</a> of the antenna's <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pattern" title="Radiation pattern">radiation pattern</a>. A high-gain antenna will radiate most of its power in a particular direction, while a low-gain antenna will radiate over a wider angle. The <i>antenna gain</i>, or <i>power gain</i> of an antenna is defined as the ratio of the <a href="/wiki/Intensity_(physics)" title="Intensity (physics)">intensity</a> (power per unit surface area) <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mstyle>

This dimensionless ratio is usually expressed <a href="/wiki/Logarithm" title="Logarithm">logarithmically</a> in <a href="/wiki/Decibel" title="Decibel">decibels</a>, these units are called "decibels-isotropic" (dBi)</p>

<p>A second unit used to measure gain is the ratio of the power radiated by the antenna to the power radiated by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half-wave_dipole" title="Half-wave dipole">half-wave dipole</a> antenna <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</dl>
<p>High-gain antennas have the advantage of longer range and better signal quality, but must be aimed carefully at the other antenna. An example of a high-gain antenna is a <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_antenna" title="Parabolic antenna">parabolic dish</a> such as a <a href="/wiki/Satellite_television" title="Satellite television">satellite television</a> antenna. Low-gain antennas have shorter range, but the orientation of the antenna is relatively unimportant. An example of a low-gain antenna is the <a href="/wiki/Whip_antenna" title="Whip antenna">whip antenna</a> found on portable radios and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cordless_phone" title="Cordless phone">cordless phones</a>. Antenna gain should not be confused with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gain_(electromagnetics)" title="Gain (electromagnetics)">amplifier gain</a>, a separate parameter measuring the increase in signal power due to an amplifying device.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effective_area_or_aperture">Effective area or aperture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Effective area or aperture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antenna_effective_area" title="Antenna effective area">Antenna effective area</a></div>
<p>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antenna_effective_area" title="Antenna effective area">effective area</a></i> or effective aperture of a receiving antenna expresses the portion of the power of a passing electromagnetic wave which it delivers to its terminals, expressed in terms of an equivalent area. For instance, if a radio wave passing a given location has a flux of 1 pW / m<sup>2</sup> (10<sup>−12</sup> watts per square meter) and an antenna has an effective area of 12 m<sup>2</sup>, then the antenna would deliver 12 pW of <a href="/wiki/Radio_frequency" title="Radio frequency">RF</a> power to the receiver (30 microvolts <a href="/wiki/Root_mean_square" title="Root mean square">rms</a> at 75 ohms). Since the receiving antenna is not equally sensitive to signals received from all directions, the effective area is a function of the direction to the source.</p>
<p>Due to <a href="/wiki/Reciprocity_(electromagnetism)" title="Reciprocity (electromagnetism)">reciprocity</a> (discussed above) the gain of an antenna used for transmitting must be proportional to its effective area when used for receiving. Consider an antenna with no <a href="/wiki/Copper_loss" title="Copper loss">loss</a>, that is, one whose <a href="/wiki/Antenna_efficiency" title="Antenna efficiency">electrical efficiency</a> is 100%. It can be shown that its effective area averaged over all directions must be equal to <span class="texhtml">λ<sup>2</sup>/4π</span>, the wavelength squared divided by <span class="texhtml">4π</span>. Gain is defined such that the average gain over all directions for an antenna with 100% <a href="/wiki/Antenna_efficiency" title="Antenna efficiency">electrical efficiency</a> is equal to 1. Therefore, the effective area <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i><sub>eff</sub></span> in terms of the gain <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">G</span> in a given direction is given by:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>A</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">f</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">f</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<msup>
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mrow>
<mn>4</mn>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mi>G</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle A_{\mathrm {eff} }={\lambda ^{2} \over 4\pi }\,G}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="A_{\mathrm{eff}} =  {\lambda^2 \over 4 \pi} \, G " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b433dd41b0c53ed0da9b4fad0324d1e70d573202" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:12.646ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>For an antenna with an <a href="/wiki/Antenna_efficiency" title="Antenna efficiency">efficiency</a> of less than 100%, both the effective area and gain are reduced by that same amount. Therefore, the above relationship between gain and effective area still holds. These are thus two different ways of expressing the same quantity. <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i><sub>eff</sub></span> is especially convenient when computing the power that would be received by an antenna of a specified gain, as illustrated by the above example.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation_pattern">Radiation pattern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Radiation pattern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pattern" title="Radiation pattern">Radiation pattern</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sidelobes_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="360" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Sidelobes_en.svg/220px-Sidelobes_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Sidelobes_en.svg/330px-Sidelobes_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Sidelobes_en.svg/440px-Sidelobes_en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sidelobes_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Polar plots of the horizontal cross sections of a (virtual) Yagi-Uda-antenna. Outline connects points with 3db field power compared to an ISO emitter.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pattern" title="Radiation pattern">radiation pattern</a> of an antenna is a plot of the relative field strength of the radio waves emitted by the antenna at different angles. It is typically represented by a three-dimensional graph, or polar plots of the horizontal and vertical cross sections. The pattern of an ideal <a href="/wiki/Isotropic_radiator" title="Isotropic radiator">isotropic antenna</a>, which radiates equally in all directions, would look like a <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">sphere</a>. Many nondirectional antennas, such as <a href="/wiki/Monopole_antenna" title="Monopole antenna">monopoles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dipole_antenna" title="Dipole antenna">dipoles</a>, emit equal power in all horizontal directions, with the power dropping off at higher and lower angles; this is called an <a href="/wiki/Omnidirectional_antenna" title="Omnidirectional antenna">omnidirectional pattern</a> and when plotted looks like a <a href="/wiki/Torus" title="Torus">torus</a> or donut.</p>
<p>The radiation of many antennas shows a pattern of maxima or "<i>lobes</i>" at various angles, separated by "<i><a href="/wiki/Null_(radio)" title="Null (radio)">nulls</a></i>", angles where the radiation falls to zero. This is because the radio waves emitted by different parts of the antenna typically <a href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interfere</a>, causing maxima at angles where the radio waves arrive at distant points <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In_phase" title="In phase">in phase</a>, and zero radiation at other angles where the radio waves arrive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Out_of_phase" title="Out of phase">out of phase</a>. In a <a href="/wiki/Directional_antenna" title="Directional antenna">directional antenna</a> designed to project radio waves in a particular direction, the lobe in that direction is designed larger than the others and is called the "<i>main lobe</i>". The other lobes usually represent unwanted radiation and are called "<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sidelobe" title="Sidelobe">sidelobes</a></i>". The axis through the main lobe is called the "<i>principal axis</i>" or "<i><a href="/wiki/Antenna_boresight" title="Antenna boresight">boresight</a> axis</i>".</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Field_regions">Field regions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Field regions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Near_and_far_field" title="Near and far field">Near and far field</a></div>
<p>The space surrounding an antenna can be divided into three concentric regions: the reactive near-field, the radiating near-field (Fresnel region) and the far-field (Fraunhofer) regions. These regions are useful to identify the field structure in each, although there are no precise boundaries.</p>
<p>The far-field region is are far enough from the antenna to neglect its size and shape. It can be assumed that the electromagnetic wave is purely a radiating plane wave (electric and magnetic fields are in phase and perpendicular to each other and to the direction of propagation). This simplifies the mathematical analysis of the radiated field.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impedance">Impedance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Impedance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As an electro-magnetic wave travels through the different parts of the antenna system (radio, feed line, antenna, free space) it may encounter differences in impedance (E/H, V/I, etc.). At each interface, depending on the impedance match, some fraction of the wave's energy will reflect back to the source,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> forming a standing wave in the feed line. The ratio of maximum power to minimum power in the wave can be measured and is called the <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave_ratio" title="Standing wave ratio">standing wave ratio</a> (SWR). A SWR of 1:1 is ideal. A SWR of 1.5:1 is considered to be marginally acceptable in low power applications where power loss is more critical, although an SWR as high as 6:1 may still be usable with the right equipment. Minimizing impedance differences at each interface (<a href="/wiki/Impedance_matching" title="Impedance matching">impedance matching</a>) will reduce SWR and maximize power transfer through each part of the antenna system.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">Complex</a> impedance of an antenna is related to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_length_(antenna)" title="Electrical length (antenna)">electrical length</a> of the antenna at the wavelength in use.
