The impedance of an antenna can be matched to the feed line and radio by adjusting the impedance of the feed line, using the feed line as an impedance <a href="/wiki/Transformer" title="Transformer">transformer</a>. More commonly, the impedance is adjusted at the load (see below) with an <a href="/wiki/Antenna_tuner" title="Antenna tuner">antenna tuner</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Balun" title="Balun">balun</a>, a matching transformer, matching networks composed of <a href="/wiki/Inductor" title="Inductor">inductors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Capacitor" title="Capacitor">capacitors</a>, or matching sections such as the gamma match.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Efficiency">Efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Efficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Antenna_efficiency" title="Antenna efficiency">Antenna efficiency</a></div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Electrical_efficiency" title="Electrical efficiency">Efficiency</a></i> of a transmitting antenna is the ratio of power actually radiated (in all directions) to the power absorbed by the antenna terminals. The power supplied to the antenna terminals which is not radiated is converted into heat. This is usually through <a href="/wiki/Copper_loss" title="Copper loss">loss resistance</a> in the antenna's conductors, but can also be due to dielectric or magnetic core losses in antennas (or antenna systems) using such components. Such loss effectively robs power from the transmitter, requiring a stronger transmitter in order to transmit a signal of a given strength.</p>
<p>For instance, if a transmitter delivers 100 W into an antenna having an efficiency of 80%, then the antenna will radiate 80 W as radio waves and produce 20 W of heat. In order to radiate 100 W of power, one would need to use a transmitter capable of supplying 125 W to the antenna. Antenna efficiency is separate from <a href="/wiki/Impedance_matching" title="Impedance matching">impedance matching</a>, which may also reduce the amount of power radiated using a given transmitter. If an <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave_ratio" title="Standing wave ratio">SWR</a> meter reads 150 W of incident power and 50 W of reflected power, that means that 100 W have actually been absorbed by the antenna (ignoring transmission line losses). How much of that power has actually been radiated cannot be directly determined through electrical measurements at (or before) the antenna terminals, but would require (for instance) careful measurement of <a href="/wiki/Field_strength" title="Field strength">field strength</a>. The loss resistance and efficiency of an antenna can be calculated.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Copper_loss" title="Copper loss">loss resistance</a> will generally affect the feedpoint impedance, adding to its resistive component. That resistance will consist of the sum of the <a href="/wiki/Radiation_resistance" title="Radiation resistance">radiation resistance</a> R<sub>r</sub> and the loss resistance R<sub>loss</sub>. If a current I is delivered to the terminals of an antenna, then a power of I<sup>2</sup>R<sub>r</sub> will be radiated and a power of I<sup>2</sup>R<sub>loss</sub> will be lost as heat. Therefore, the efficiency of an antenna is equal to R<sub>r</sub> / (R<sub>r</sub> + R<sub>loss</sub>). Only the total resistance R<sub>r</sub> + R<sub>loss</sub> can be directly measured.</p>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Reciprocity_(electromagnetism)" title="Reciprocity (electromagnetism)">reciprocity</a>, the efficiency of an antenna used as a receiving antenna is identical to the efficiency as defined above. The power that an antenna will deliver to a receiver (with a proper <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impedance_match" title="Impedance match">impedance match</a>) is reduced by the same amount. In some receiving applications, the very inefficient antennas may have little impact on performance. At low frequencies, for example, atmospheric or man-made noise can mask antenna inefficiency. For example, CCIR Rep. 258-3 indicates man-made noise in a residential setting at 40 MHz is about 28 dB above the thermal noise floor. Consequently, an antenna with a 20 dB loss (due to inefficiency) would have little impact on system noise performance. The loss within the antenna will affect the intended signal and the noise/interference identically, leading to no reduction in signal to noise ratio (SNR).</p>
<p>Antennas which are not a good fraction of a wavelength in size are inevitably inefficient due to their small radiation resistance. AM broadcast radios include a small <a href="/wiki/Loop_antenna#AM_broadcast_receiver_loop_antennas" title="Loop antenna">loop antenna</a> for reception which has an extremely poor efficiency. This has little effect on the receiver's performance, but simply requires greater amplification by the receiver's electronics. Contrast this tiny component to the massive and very tall towers used at AM broadcast stations for transmitting at the very same frequency, where every percentage point of reduced antenna efficiency entails a substantial cost.</p>
<p>The definition of <a href="/wiki/Antenna_gain" title="Antenna gain">antenna gain</a> or <i>power gain</i> already includes the effect of the antenna's efficiency. Therefore, if one is trying to radiate a signal toward a receiver using a transmitter of a given power, one need only compare the gain of various antennas rather than considering the efficiency as well. This is likewise true for a receiving antenna at very high (especially microwave) frequencies, where the point is to receive a signal which is strong compared to the receiver's noise temperature. However, in the case of a directional antenna used for receiving signals with the intention of <i>rejecting</i> interference from different directions, one is no longer concerned with the antenna efficiency, as discussed above. In this case, rather than quoting the <a href="/wiki/Antenna_gain" title="Antenna gain">antenna gain</a>, one would be more concerned with the <i>directive gain</i> which does <i>not</i> include the effect of antenna (in)efficiency. The directive gain of an antenna can be computed from the published gain divided by the antenna's efficiency.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polarization">Polarization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Polarization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)#Antennas" title="Polarization (waves)">Polarization (waves) § Antennas</a></div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a></i> of an antenna refers to the orientation of the electric field (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E-plane" title="E-plane">E-plane</a>) of the radio wave with respect to the Earth's surface and is determined by the physical structure of the antenna and by its orientation. This is distinct from the antenna's directionality. Thus, a simple straight wire antenna will have one polarization when mounted vertically, and a different polarization when mounted horizontally. As a <a href="/wiki/Transverse_wave" title="Transverse wave">transverse wave</a>, the magnetic field of a radio wave is at right angles to that of the electric field, but by convention, talk of an antenna's "polarization" is understood to refer to the direction of the electric field.</p>
<p>Reflections generally affect polarization. For radio waves, one important reflector is the <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a> which can change the wave's polarization. Thus for signals received following reflection by the ionosphere (a <a href="/wiki/Skywave" title="Skywave">skywave</a>), a consistent polarization cannot be expected. For <a href="/wiki/Line-of-sight_propagation" title="Line-of-sight propagation">line-of-sight communications</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_wave" title="Ground wave">ground wave</a> propagation, horizontally or vertically polarized transmissions generally remain in about the same polarization state at the receiving location. Matching the receiving antenna's polarization to that of the transmitter can make a very substantial difference in received signal strength.</p>
<p>Polarization is predictable from an antenna's geometry, although in some cases it is not at all obvious (such as for the <a href="/wiki/Quad_antenna" title="Quad antenna">quad antenna</a>). An antenna's linear polarization is generally along the direction (as viewed from the receiving location) of the antenna's currents when such a direction can be defined. For instance, a vertical <a href="/wiki/Whip_antenna" title="Whip antenna">whip antenna</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wi-Fi" title="Wi-Fi">Wi-Fi</a> antenna vertically oriented will transmit and receive in the vertical polarization. Antennas with horizontal elements, such as most rooftop TV antennas in the United States, are horizontally polarized (broadcast TV in the U.S. usually uses horizontal polarization). Even when the antenna system has a vertical orientation, such as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antenna_array_(electromagnetic)" title="Antenna array (electromagnetic)">array</a> of horizontal dipole antennas, the polarization is in the horizontal direction corresponding to the current flow. The polarization of a commercial antenna is an essential specification.</p>
<p>Polarization is the sum of the E-plane orientations over time projected onto an imaginary plane perpendicular to the direction of motion of the radio wave. In the most general case, polarization is <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">elliptical</a>, meaning that the polarization of the radio waves varies over time. Two special cases are <a href="/wiki/Linear_polarization" title="Linear polarization">linear polarization</a> (the ellipse collapses into a line) as discussed above, and <a href="/wiki/Circular_polarization" title="Circular polarization">circular polarization</a> (in which the two axes of the ellipse are equal). In linear polarization the electric field of the radio wave oscillates back and forth along one direction; this can be affected by the mounting of the antenna but usually the desired direction is either horizontal or vertical polarization. In circular polarization, the electric field (and magnetic field) of the radio wave rotates at the radio frequency circularly around the axis of propagation. Circular or elliptically polarized radio waves are <a href="/wiki/Circular_polarization#Left.2Fright_handedness_conventions" title="Circular polarization">designated as right-handed or left-handed</a> using the "thumb in the direction of the propagation" rule. For circular polarization, optical researchers use the opposite <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_hand_rule" title="Right hand rule">right hand rule</a> from the one used by radio engineers.</p>
<p>It is best for the receiving antenna to match the polarization of the transmitted wave for optimum reception. Intermediate matchings will lose some signal strength, but not as much as a complete mismatch. A circularly polarized antenna can be used to equally well match vertical or horizontal linear polarizations. Transmission from a circularly polarized antenna received by a linearly polarized antenna (or vice versa) entails a 3 dB reduction in <a href="/wiki/Signal-to-noise_ratio" title="Signal-to-noise ratio">signal-to-noise ratio</a> as the received power has thereby been cut in half.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impedance_matching">Impedance matching</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Impedance matching">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Impedance_matching" title="Impedance matching">Impedance matching</a></div>
<p>Maximum power transfer requires matching the impedance of an antenna system (as seen looking into the transmission line) to the <a href="/wiki/Complex_conjugate" title="Complex conjugate">complex conjugate</a> of the impedance of the receiver or transmitter. In the case of a transmitter, however, the desired matching impedance might not correspond to the dynamic output impedance of the transmitter as analyzed as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thevenin%27s_theorem" title="Thevenin's theorem">source impedance</a> but rather the design value (typically 50 ohms) required for efficient and safe operation of the transmitting circuitry. The intended impedance is normally resistive but a transmitter (and some receivers) may have additional adjustments to cancel a certain amount of reactance in order to "tweak" the match. When a transmission line is used in between the antenna and the transmitter (or receiver) one generally would like an antenna system whose impedance is resistive and near the <a href="/wiki/Characteristic_impedance" title="Characteristic impedance">characteristic impedance</a> of that transmission line in order to minimize the <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave_ratio" title="Standing wave ratio">standing wave ratio</a> (SWR) and the increase in transmission line losses it entails, in addition to supplying a good match at the transmitter or receiver itself.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Antenna_tuner" title="Antenna tuner">Antenna tuning</a> generally refers to cancellation of any reactance seen at the antenna terminals, leaving only a resistive impedance which might or might not be exactly the desired impedance (that of the transmission line). Although an antenna may be designed to have a purely resistive feedpoint impedance (such as a dipole 97% of a half wavelength long) this might not be exactly true at the frequency that it is eventually used at. In some cases the physical length of the antenna can be "trimmed" to obtain a pure resistance. On the other hand, the addition of a series inductance or parallel capacitance can be used to cancel a residual capacitative or inductive reactance, respectively.</p>
<p>In some cases this is done in a more extreme manner, not simply to cancel a small amount of residual reactance, but to resonate an antenna whose resonance frequency is quite different from the intended frequency of operation. For instance, a "whip antenna" can be made significantly shorter than 1/4 wavelength long, for practical reasons, and then resonated using a so-called <a href="/wiki/Loading_coil#Radio_antenna" title="Loading coil">loading coil</a>. This physically large inductor at the base of the antenna has an inductive reactance which is the opposite of the capacitative reactance that such a vertical antenna has at the desired operating frequency. The result is a pure resistance seen at feedpoint of the loading coil; that resistance is somewhat lower than would be desired to match commercial <a href="/wiki/Coaxial_cable" title="Coaxial cable">coax</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>So an additional problem beyond canceling the unwanted reactance is of matching the remaining resistive impedance to the <a href="/wiki/Characteristic_impedance" title="Characteristic impedance">characteristic impedance</a> of the transmission line. In principle this can be done with a transformer, however the turns ratio of a transformer is not adjustable. A general matching network with at least two adjustments can be made to correct both components of impedance. Matching networks using discrete inductors and capacitors will have losses associated with those components, and will have power restrictions when used for transmitting. Avoiding these difficulties, commercial antennas are generally designed with fixed matching elements or feeding strategies to get an approximate match to standard coax, such as 50 or 75 ohms. Antennas based on the dipole (rather than vertical antennas) may include a <a href="/wiki/Balun" title="Balun">balun</a> between the transmission line and antenna element, which may be integrated into any such matching network.</p>
<p>Another extreme case of impedance matching occurs when using a small <a href="/wiki/Loop_antenna" title="Loop antenna">loop antenna</a> (usually, but not always, for receiving) at a relatively low frequency where it appears almost as a pure inductor. Resonating such an inductor with a capacitor at the frequency of operation not only cancels the reactance but greatly magnifies the very small <a href="/wiki/Radiation_resistance" title="Radiation resistance">radiation resistance</a> of such a loop.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This is implemented in most AM broadcast receivers, with a small ferrite loop antenna resonated by a capacitor which is varied along with the receiver tuning in order to maintain resonance over the AM broadcast band</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Antenna_types">Antenna types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Antenna types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Antennas can be classified in various ways. The list below groups together antennas under common operating principles, following the way antennas are classified in many engineering textbooks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bevelaqua1_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bevelaqua1-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aksoy_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aksoy-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>Isotropic</b>: An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotropic_antenna" title="Isotropic antenna">isotropic antenna</a> (<a href="/wiki/Isotropic_radiator" title="Isotropic radiator">isotropic radiator</a>) is a <i>hypothetical</i> antenna that radiates equal signal power in all directions. It is a <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_model" title="Mathematical model">mathematical model</a> that is used as the base of comparison to calculate the gain of real antennas. No real antenna can have an isotropic <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pattern" title="Radiation pattern">radiation pattern</a>. However <i>approximately</i> isotropic antennas, constructed with multiple elements, are used in antenna testing.</p>
<p>The first four groups below are usually <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resonant" title="Resonant">resonant</a> antennas; when driven at their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resonant_frequency" title="Resonant frequency">resonant frequency</a> their elements act as <a href="/wiki/Resonator" title="Resonator">resonators</a>. Waves of current and voltage bounce back and forth between the ends, creating <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave" title="Standing wave">standing waves</a> along the elements.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dipole">Dipole</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Dipole">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rabbit-ears_dipole_antenna_with_UHF_loop_20090204.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2291" data-file-width="1672" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Rabbit-ears_dipole_antenna_with_UHF_loop_20090204.jpg/150px-Rabbit-ears_dipole_antenna_with_UHF_loop_20090204.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Rabbit-ears_dipole_antenna_with_UHF_loop_20090204.jpg/225px-Rabbit-ears_dipole_antenna_with_UHF_loop_20090204.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Rabbit-ears_dipole_antenna_with_UHF_loop_20090204.jpg/300px-Rabbit-ears_dipole_antenna_with_UHF_loop_20090204.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rabbit-ears_dipole_antenna_with_UHF_loop_20090204.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Rabbit ears" <a href="/wiki/Dipole_antenna" title="Dipole antenna">dipole</a> antenna for VHF television reception</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dipole_antenna" title="Dipole antenna">dipole</a> is the prototypical antenna on which a large class of antennas are based. A basic dipole antenna consists of two conductors (usually metal rods or wires) arranged symmetrically, with one side of the balanced feedline from the transmitter or receiver attached to each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aksoy_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aksoy-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bevelaqua3_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bevelaqua3-18">[18]</a></sup> The most common type, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half-wave_dipole" title="Half-wave dipole">half-wave dipole</a>, consists of two resonant elements just under a quarter wavelength long. This antenna radiates maximally in directions perpendicular to the antenna's axis, giving it a small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Directive_gain" title="Directive gain">directive gain</a> of 2.15 dBi. Although half-wave dipoles are used alone as omnidirectional antennas, they are also a building block of many other more complicated directional antennas.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yagi_TV_antenna_1954.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="302" data-file-width="433" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Yagi_TV_antenna_1954.png/150px-Yagi_TV_antenna_1954.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Yagi_TV_antenna_1954.png/225px-Yagi_TV_antenna_1954.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Yagi_TV_antenna_1954.png/300px-Yagi_TV_antenna_1954.png 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yagi_TV_antenna_1954.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yagi-Uda_antenna" title="Yagi-Uda antenna">Yagi-Uda</a> <a href="/wiki/Television_antenna" title="Television antenna">television antenna</a> for analog channels 2-4, 47-68 MHz</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yagi-Uda_antenna" title="Yagi-Uda antenna">Yagi-Uda</a></i> – One of the most common directional antennas at HF, VHF, and UHF frequencies. Consists of multiple half wave dipole elements in a line, with a single <a href="/wiki/Driven_element" title="Driven element">driven element</a> and multiple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasitic_element" title="Parasitic element">parasitic elements</a> which serve to create a uni-directional or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beam_antenna" title="Beam antenna">beam</a> antenna. These typically have gains between 10–20 dBi depending on the number of elements used, and are very narrowband (with a usable bandwidth of only a few percent) though there are derivative designs which relax this limitation. Used for rooftop <a href="/wiki/Television_antenna" title="Television antenna">television antennas</a>, point-to-point communication links, and long distance <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shortwave" title="Shortwave">shortwave</a> communication using <a href="/wiki/Skywave" title="Skywave">skywave</a> ("skip") reflection from the ionosphere.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VHF_UHF_LP-antenna_closeup.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1194" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/VHF_UHF_LP-antenna_closeup.JPG/150px-VHF_UHF_LP-antenna_closeup.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/VHF_UHF_LP-antenna_closeup.JPG/225px-VHF_UHF_LP-antenna_closeup.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/VHF_UHF_LP-antenna_closeup.JPG/300px-VHF_UHF_LP-antenna_closeup.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VHF_UHF_LP-antenna_closeup.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Log-periodic_dipole_array" title="Log-periodic dipole array">Log-periodic dipole array</a> covering 140-470 MHz</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Log-periodic_dipole_array" title="Log-periodic dipole array">Log-periodic dipole array</a></i> – Often confused with the Yagi-Uda, this consists of many dipole elements along a boom with gradually increasing lengths, all connected to the transmission line with alternating polarity. It is a directional antenna with a wide bandwidth. This makes it ideal for use as a rooftop television antenna, although its gain is much less than a Yagi of comparable size.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SatelliteAntenna-137MHz_closeup.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="634" data-file-width="490" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/SatelliteAntenna-137MHz_closeup.jpg/150px-SatelliteAntenna-137MHz_closeup.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/SatelliteAntenna-137MHz_closeup.jpg/225px-SatelliteAntenna-137MHz_closeup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/SatelliteAntenna-137MHz_closeup.jpg/300px-SatelliteAntenna-137MHz_closeup.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SatelliteAntenna-137MHz_closeup.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Two-element <a href="/wiki/Turnstile_antenna" title="Turnstile antenna">turnstile antenna</a> for reception of weather satellite data, 137 MHz. Has circular polarization.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Turnstile_antenna" title="Turnstile antenna">Turnstile</a></i> – Two dipole antennas mounted at right angles, fed with a phase difference of 90°. This antenna is unusual in that it radiates in <i>all</i> directions (no nulls in the radiation pattern), with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horizontal_polarization" title="Horizontal polarization">horizontal polarization</a> in directions coplanar with the elements, <a href="/wiki/Circular_polarization" title="Circular polarization">circular polarization</a> normal to that plane, and elliptical polarization in other directions. Used for receiving signals from satellites, as circular polarization is transmitted by many satellites.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corner_reflector_TV_antenna.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="339" data-file-width="197" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Corner_reflector_TV_antenna.png/150px-Corner_reflector_TV_antenna.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ec/Corner_reflector_TV_antenna.png 1.5x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corner_reflector_TV_antenna.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Corner_reflector_antenna" title="Corner reflector antenna">Corner reflector</a> UHF TV antenna with "bowtie" dipole driven element</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Corner_reflector_antenna" title="Corner reflector antenna">Corner reflector</a></i> – A directive antenna with moderate gain of about 8 dBi often used at UHF frequencies. Consists of a dipole mounted in front of two reflective metal screens joined at an angle, usually 90°. Used as a rooftop UHF television antenna and for point-to-point data links.</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Patch_antenna" title="Patch antenna">Patch</a> (<a href="/wiki/Microstrip_antenna" title="Microstrip antenna">microstrip</a>)</i> – A type of antenna with elements consisting of metal sheets mounted over a ground plane. Similar to dipole with gain of 6–9 dBi. Integrated into surfaces such as aircraft bodies. Their easy fabrication using <a href="/wiki/Printed_circuit_board" title="Printed circuit board">PCB</a> techniques have made them popular in modern wireless devices. Often combined into arrays.</li>
</ul>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monopole">Monopole</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Monopole">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Monopole_antenna" title="Monopole antenna">Monopole antennas</a> consist of a single conductor such as a metal rod, mounted over the ground or an artificial conducting surface (a so-called <i><a href="/wiki/Ground_plane" title="Ground plane">ground plane</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aksoy_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Aksoy-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bevelaqua2_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bevelaqua2-19">[19]</a></sup> One side of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feedline" title="Feedline">feedline</a> from the receiver or transmitter is connected to the conductor, and the other side to ground and/or the artificial ground plane. The monopole is best understood as a dipole antenna in which one conductor is omitted; the radiation is generated as if the second arm of the dipole were present due to the effective <a href="/wiki/Image_antenna" title="Image antenna">image current</a> seen as a reflection of the monopole from the ground. Since all of the equivalent dipole's radiation is concentrated in a half-space, the antenna has twice (3 dB increase of) the gain of a similar dipole, not considering losses in the ground plane.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Klaudia_801.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Klaudia_801.JPG/150px-Klaudia_801.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Klaudia_801.JPG/225px-Klaudia_801.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Klaudia_801.JPG/300px-Klaudia_801.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Klaudia_801.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Quarter-wave <a href="/wiki/Whip_antenna" title="Whip antenna">whip antenna</a> on an FM radio for 88-108 MHz</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The most common form is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quarter-wave_antenna" title="Quarter-wave antenna">quarter-wave monopole</a> which is one-quarter of a <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> long and has a gain of 5.12 dBi when mounted over a ground plane. Monopoles have an <a href="/wiki/Omnidirectional_antenna" title="Omnidirectional antenna">omnidirectional</a> <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pattern" title="Radiation pattern">radiation pattern</a>, so they are used for broad coverage of an area, and have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertical_polarization" title="Vertical polarization">vertical polarization</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_wave" title="Ground wave">ground waves</a> used for broadcasting at low frequencies must be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertical_polarization" title="Vertical polarization">vertically polarized</a>, so large vertical monopole antennas are used for broadcasting in the MF, LF, and VLF bands. Small monopoles are used as nondirectional antennas on portable radios in the HF, VHF, and UHF bands.</p>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Whip_antenna" title="Whip antenna">Whip</a></i> – Type of antenna used on mobile and portable radios in the VHF and UHF bands such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boom_box" title="Boom box">boom boxes</a>, consists of a flexible rod, often made of telescoping segments.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uhf_cb_wit_rubber_ducky_exposed.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Uhf_cb_wit_rubber_ducky_exposed.jpg/150px-Uhf_cb_wit_rubber_ducky_exposed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Uhf_cb_wit_rubber_ducky_exposed.jpg/225px-Uhf_cb_wit_rubber_ducky_exposed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Uhf_cb_wit_rubber_ducky_exposed.jpg/300px-Uhf_cb_wit_rubber_ducky_exposed.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uhf_cb_wit_rubber_ducky_exposed.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rubber_Ducky_antenna" title="Rubber Ducky antenna">Rubber Ducky antenna</a> on UHF 446 MHz <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walkie_talkie" title="Walkie talkie">walkie talkie</a> with rubber cover removed.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Rubber_ducky_antenna" title="Rubber ducky antenna">Rubber Ducky</a></i> – Most common antenna used on portable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two_way_radio" title="Two way radio">two way radios</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cordless_phone" title="Cordless phone">cordless phones</a> due to its compactness, consists of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrically_short" title="Electrically short">electrically short</a> wire <a href="/wiki/Helix" title="Helix">helix</a>. The helix adds <a href="/wiki/Inductance" title="Inductance">inductance</a> to cancel the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capacitive_reactance" title="Capacitive reactance">capacitive reactance</a> of the short radiator, making it resonant. Very low gain.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antenne_gp_vhf_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1140" data-file-width="744" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Antenne_gp_vhf_3.jpg/150px-Antenne_gp_vhf_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Antenne_gp_vhf_3.jpg/225px-Antenne_gp_vhf_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Antenne_gp_vhf_3.jpg/300px-Antenne_gp_vhf_3.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antenne_gp_vhf_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
VHF ground plane antenna</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Whip_antenna" title="Whip antenna">Ground plane</a></i> – a whip antenna with several rods extending horizontally from base of whip attached to the ground side of the feedline. Since whips are mounted above ground, the horizontal rods form an artificial <a href="/wiki/Ground_plane" title="Ground plane">ground plane</a> under the antenna to increase its gain. Used as base station antennas for <a href="/wiki/Land_mobile_radio_system" title="Land mobile radio system">land mobile radio systems</a> such as police, ambulance and taxi dispatchers.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sendemast_Hirschlanden.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2660" data-file-width="1995" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Sendemast_Hirschlanden.jpg/150px-Sendemast_Hirschlanden.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Sendemast_Hirschlanden.jpg/225px-Sendemast_Hirschlanden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Sendemast_Hirschlanden.jpg/300px-Sendemast_Hirschlanden.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sendemast_Hirschlanden.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mast_radiator" title="Mast radiator">Mast radiator</a> antenna of <a href="/wiki/Medium_wave" title="Medium wave">medium wave</a> AM radio station, Germany</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Mast_radiator" title="Mast radiator">Mast radiator</a></i> – A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_tower" title="Radio tower">radio tower</a> in which the tower structure itself serves as the antenna. Common form of transmitting antenna for <a href="/wiki/Radio_station" title="Radio station">AM radio stations</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medium_Frequency" title="Medium Frequency">MF</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_Frequency" title="Low Frequency">LF</a> transmitters. At its base the tower is usually, but not necessarily, mounted on a ceramic <a href="/wiki/Insulator_(electricity)" title="Insulator (electricity)">insulator</a> to isolate it from the ground.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amateur_T_cage_antenna_2BML_1922.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1049" data-file-width="1277" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Amateur_T_cage_antenna_2BML_1922.jpg/150px-Amateur_T_cage_antenna_2BML_1922.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Amateur_T_cage_antenna_2BML_1922.jpg/225px-Amateur_T_cage_antenna_2BML_1922.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Amateur_T_cage_antenna_2BML_1922.jpg/300px-Amateur_T_cage_antenna_2BML_1922.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amateur_T_cage_antenna_2BML_1922.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T_antenna" title="T antenna">T antenna</a> of amateur radio station, 80 ft high, used at 1.5 MHz.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T_antenna" title="T antenna">T and inverted L</a></i> – Consist of a long horizontal wire suspended between two towers with insulators, with a vertical wire hanging down from it, attached to a feedline to the receiver or transmitter. Used on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_Frequency" title="Low Frequency">LF</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Very_Low_Frequency" title="Very Low Frequency">VLF</a> bands. The vertical wire serves as the radiator. Since at these frequencies the vertical wire is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrically_short" title="Electrically short">electrically short</a>, much shorter than a quarter wavelength, the horizontal wire(s) serve as a capacitive "hat" to increase the current in the vertical radiator, increasing the gain. Very narrow bandwidth, requires <a href="/wiki/Loading_coil" title="Loading coil">loading coil</a> to tune out any remaining capacitive reactance. Requires low resistance <a href="/wiki/Ground_(electricity)" title="Ground (electricity)">ground (electricity)</a>.</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Inverted-F_antenna" title="Inverted-F antenna"><i>Inverted F</i></a> – Combines the advantages of the compactness of inverted-L antenna, and the good matching of the F-type antenna. The antenna is grounded at the base and fed at some intermediate point. The position of the feed point determines the antenna impedance. Thus, matching can be achieved without the need for an extraneous matching network.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Umbrella_antenna" title="Umbrella antenna">Umbrella</a></i> – Very large wire transmitting antennas used on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Very_Low_Frequency" title="Very Low Frequency">VLF</a> bands. Consists of a central <a href="/wiki/Mast_radiator" title="Mast radiator">mast radiator</a> tower attached at the top to multiple wires extending out radially from the mast to ground, like a tent or umbrella, insulated at the ends. Extremely narrow bandwidth, requires large loading coil and low resistance <a href="/wiki/Counterpoise_(ground_system)" title="Counterpoise (ground system)">counterpoise</a> ground. Used for long range military communications.</li>
</ul>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Array">Array</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Array">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:72px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Collinear_folded_dipole_antenna_array.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="784" data-file-width="264" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Collinear_folded_dipole_antenna_array.jpg/70px-Collinear_folded_dipole_antenna_array.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Collinear_folded_dipole_antenna_array.jpg/105px-Collinear_folded_dipole_antenna_array.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Collinear_folded_dipole_antenna_array.jpg/140px-Collinear_folded_dipole_antenna_array.jpg 2x" width="70"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Collinear_folded_dipole_antenna_array.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
VHF <a href="/wiki/Collinear_antenna_array" title="Collinear antenna array">collinear array</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folded_dipole" title="Folded dipole">folded dipoles</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antenna_array_(electromagnetic)" title="Antenna array (electromagnetic)">Array antennas</a></i> consist of multiple antennas working as a single antenna. Typically they consist of arrays of identical <a href="/wiki/Driven_element" title="Driven element">driven elements</a>, usually dipoles fed in phase, giving increased gain over that of a single dipole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aksoy_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Aksoy-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bevelaqua4_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bevelaqua4-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Collinear_antenna_array" title="Collinear antenna array">Collinear</a></i> - Consist of a number of dipoles in a vertical line. It is a high gain omnidirectional antenna, meaning more of the power is radiated in horizontal directions and less into the sky or ground and wasted. Gain of 8 to 10 dBi. Used as base station antennas for <a href="/wiki/Land_mobile_radio_system" title="Land mobile radio system">land mobile radio systems</a> such as police, fire, ambulance, and taxi dispatchers, and <a href="/wiki/Sector_antenna" title="Sector antenna">sector antennas</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cellular_base_station" title="Cellular base station">cellular base stations</a>.</li>
</ul>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eight_bay_bowtie_TV_antenna.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1448" data-file-width="972" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Eight_bay_bowtie_TV_antenna.jpg/150px-Eight_bay_bowtie_TV_antenna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Eight_bay_bowtie_TV_antenna.jpg/225px-Eight_bay_bowtie_TV_antenna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Eight_bay_bowtie_TV_antenna.jpg/300px-Eight_bay_bowtie_TV_antenna.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eight_bay_bowtie_TV_antenna.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Reflective_array_antenna" title="Reflective array antenna">Reflective array</a> UHF TV antenna, with 8 bowtie dipoles to cover the UHF 470-890 MHz band</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Reflective_array_antenna" title="Reflective array antenna">Reflective array</a></i> - multiple dipoles in a two-dimensional array mounted in front of a flat reflecting screen. Used for radar and UHF television transmitting and receiving antennas.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PAVE_PAWS_Radar_Clear_AFS_Alaska.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="311" data-file-width="354" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/PAVE_PAWS_Radar_Clear_AFS_Alaska.jpg/150px-PAVE_PAWS_Radar_Clear_AFS_Alaska.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/PAVE_PAWS_Radar_Clear_AFS_Alaska.jpg/225px-PAVE_PAWS_Radar_Clear_AFS_Alaska.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/PAVE_PAWS_Radar_Clear_AFS_Alaska.jpg/300px-PAVE_PAWS_Radar_Clear_AFS_Alaska.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PAVE_PAWS_Radar_Clear_AFS_Alaska.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
US Air Force <a href="/wiki/PAVE_PAWS" title="PAVE PAWS">PAVE PAWS</a> <a href="/wiki/Phased_array" title="Phased array">phased array</a> radar antenna for ballistic missile detection, Alaska. The two circular arrays are each composed of 2677 crossed dipole antennas.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Phased_array" title="Phased array">Phased array</a></i> - A high gain antenna used at UHF and microwave frequencies which is electronically steerable. It consists of multiple dipoles in a two-dimensional array, each fed through an electronic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phase_shifter" title="Phase shifter">phase shifter</a>, with the phase shifters controlled by a computer control system. The beam can be instantly pointed in any direction over a wide angle in front of the antenna. Used for military <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronic_countermeasures" title="Electronic countermeasures">jamming</a> systems.</li>
</ul>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moosbrunn_SW_Antenna.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1048" data-file-width="1192" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Moosbrunn_SW_Antenna.jpg/150px-Moosbrunn_SW_Antenna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Moosbrunn_SW_Antenna.jpg/225px-Moosbrunn_SW_Antenna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Moosbrunn_SW_Antenna.jpg/300px-Moosbrunn_SW_Antenna.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moosbrunn_SW_Antenna.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Curtain_array" title="Curtain array">Curtain array</a> shortwave transmitting antenna, Austria. Wire dipoles suspended between towers</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Curtain_array" title="Curtain array">Curtain array</a></i> - Large directional wire transmitting antenna used at HF by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shortwave" title="Shortwave">shortwave</a> broadcasting stations. It consists of a vertical rectangular array of wire dipoles suspended in front of a flat reflector screen consisting of a vertical "curtain" of parallel wires, all supported between two metal towers. It radiates a horizontal beam of radio waves into the sky above the horizon, which is reflected by the ionosphere to Earth beyond the horizon</li>
</ul>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Superturnstile_Tx_Muehlacker.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1023" data-file-width="641" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Superturnstile_Tx_Muehlacker.JPG/150px-Superturnstile_Tx_Muehlacker.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Superturnstile_Tx_Muehlacker.JPG/225px-Superturnstile_Tx_Muehlacker.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Superturnstile_Tx_Muehlacker.JPG/300px-Superturnstile_Tx_Muehlacker.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Superturnstile_Tx_Muehlacker.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Batwing_antenna" title="Batwing antenna">Batwing</a> VHF television broadcasting antenna</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Batwing_antenna" title="Batwing antenna">Batwing</a></i> or <i>superturnstile</i> - A specialized antenna used in television broadcasting consisting of perpendicular pairs of dipoles with radiators resembling bat wings. Multiple batwing antennas are stacked vertically on a mast to make VHF television broadcast antennas. Omnidirectional radiation pattern with high gain in horizontal directions. The batwing shape gives them wide bandwidth.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Feedingstrips.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="997" data-file-width="1494" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Feedingstrips.jpg/150px-Feedingstrips.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Feedingstrips.jpg/225px-Feedingstrips.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Feedingstrips.jpg/300px-Feedingstrips.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Feedingstrips.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Flat <a href="/wiki/Microstrip_antenna" title="Microstrip antenna">microstrip array</a> antenna for <a href="/wiki/Satellite_television" title="Satellite television">satellite TV</a> reception</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Microstrip_antenna" title="Microstrip antenna">Microstrip</a></i> - an array of <a href="/wiki/Patch_antenna" title="Patch antenna">patch antennas</a> on a substrate fed by <a href="/wiki/Microstrip" title="Microstrip">microstrip</a> feedlines. Microwave antenna that can achieve large gains in compact space. Ease of fabrication by <a href="/wiki/Printed_circuit_board" title="Printed circuit board">PCB</a> techniques have made them popular in modern wireless devices. Beamwidth and polarization can be actively reconfigurable.</li>
</ul>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loop">Loop</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Loop">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:248px;max-width:248px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:122px;max-width:122px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loop_antenna.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Loop_antenna.jpg/120px-Loop_antenna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Loop_antenna.jpg/180px-Loop_antenna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Loop_antenna.jpg/240px-Loop_antenna.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Loop antenna for transmitting at <a href="/wiki/High_frequency" title="High frequency">high frequencies</a>, 2m diameter</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:122px;max-width:122px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loop_Antenna.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1600" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Loop_Antenna.jpg/120px-Loop_Antenna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Loop_Antenna.jpg/180px-Loop_Antenna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Loop_Antenna.jpg/240px-Loop_Antenna.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Separate loop antenna for AM radio</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Loop_antenna" title="Loop antenna">Loop antennas</a> consist of a loop (or <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_coil" title="Electromagnetic coil">coil</a>) of wire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aksoy_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-Aksoy-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bevelaqua5_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bevelaqua5-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Loops with circumference of a wavelength (or integer multiple of the wavelength) are resonant and act somewhat similarly to the half-wave dipole. However a loop small in comparison to the wavelength, also called a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_loop" title="Magnetic loop">magnetic loop</a>, performs quite differently. This antenna interacts directly with the <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> of the radio wave, making it relatively insensitive to nearby electrical noise. However it has a very small <a href="/wiki/Radiation_resistance" title="Radiation resistance">radiation resistance</a>, typically much smaller than the <a href="/wiki/Copper_loss" title="Copper loss">loss resistance</a>, making it <a href="/wiki/Antenna_efficiency" title="Antenna efficiency">inefficient</a> and thus undesirable for transmitting. They are used as receiving antennas at low frequencies, and also as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_direction-finding" title="Radio direction-finding">direction finding</a> antennas.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ferritantenne_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="320" data-file-width="2128" height="44" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Ferritantenne_2.jpg/290px-Ferritantenne_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Ferritantenne_2.jpg/435px-Ferritantenne_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Ferritantenne_2.jpg/580px-Ferritantenne_2.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ferritantenne_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ferrite <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loopstick_antenna" title="Loopstick antenna">loopstick antenna</a> from an AM broadcast radio, about 4 in (10 cm) long. The antenna is <a href="/wiki/Inductor" title="Inductor">inductive</a> and, in conjunction with a <a href="/wiki/Variable_capacitor" title="Variable capacitor">variable capacitor</a>, forms the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuned_circuit" title="Tuned circuit">tuned circuit</a> at the input stage of the receiver.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loopstick_antenna" title="Loopstick antenna">Ferrite (loopstick)</a></i> - These are used as the receiving antenna in most consumer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AM_radio" title="AM radio">AM radios</a> operating in the <a href="/wiki/Medium_wave" title="Medium wave">medium wave</a> broadcast band (and lower frequencies), a notable exception being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Car_radio" title="Car radio">car radios</a>. Wire is coiled around a <a href="/wiki/Ferrite_core" title="Ferrite core">ferrite core</a> which greatly increases the coil's <a href="/wiki/Inductance" title="Inductance">inductance</a>. Radiation pattern is maximum at directions perpendicular to the ferrite rod. The null direction of ferrite core antennas are bi-directional and sharper than the maximal directionality. This often makes the null direction more useful than the maximal directionality because the null direction can be used to reject unwanted signals from interferring stations, sometimes allowing reception from stations with weaker signals than interferring stations.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quad-antenna.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="135" data-file-width="180" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fe/Quad-antenna.png" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quad-antenna.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A two-element <a href="/wiki/Quad_antenna" title="Quad antenna">quad antenna</a> used by an <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio" title="Amateur radio">amateur radio</a> station</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Quad_antenna" title="Quad antenna">Quad</a></i> - consists of multiple wire loops in a line with one functioning as the <a href="/wiki/Driven_element" title="Driven element">driven element</a>, and the others as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasitic_element" title="Parasitic element">parasitic elements</a>. Used as a directional antenna on the <a href="/wiki/High_frequency" title="High frequency">HF</a> bands for shortwave communication.</li>
</ul>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aperture">Aperture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Aperture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:134px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BocinaLenteDiel%C3%A9ctrica.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="102" data-file-width="132" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8a/BocinaLenteDiel%C3%A9ctrica.JPG" width="132"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BocinaLenteDiel%C3%A9ctrica.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dielectric lens antenna used in millimeter wave radio telescope</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Aperture antennas are the main type of directional antennas used at <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> frequencies and above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aksoy_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-Aksoy-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> They consist of a small dipole or loop <a href="/wiki/Antenna_feed" title="Antenna feed">feed antenna</a> inside a three-dimensional guiding structure large compared to a wavelength, with an aperture to emit the radio waves. Since the antenna structure itself is nonresonant they can be used over a wide frequency range by replacing or tuning the feed antenna.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Canberra_Deep_Dish_Communications_Complex_-_GPN-2000-000502.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2394" data-file-width="2526" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Canberra_Deep_Dish_Communications_Complex_-_GPN-2000-000502.jpg/150px-Canberra_Deep_Dish_Communications_Complex_-_GPN-2000-000502.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Canberra_Deep_Dish_Communications_Complex_-_GPN-2000-000502.jpg/225px-Canberra_Deep_Dish_Communications_Complex_-_GPN-2000-000502.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Canberra_Deep_Dish_Communications_Complex_-_GPN-2000-000502.jpg/300px-Canberra_Deep_Dish_Communications_Complex_-_GPN-2000-000502.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Canberra_Deep_Dish_Communications_Complex_-_GPN-2000-000502.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
NASA <a href="/wiki/Cassegrain_antenna" title="Cassegrain antenna">Cassegrain</a> <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_antenna" title="Parabolic antenna">parabolic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spacecraft_communication" title="Spacecraft communication">spacecraft communication</a> antenna, Australia. Uses <a href="/wiki/X_band" title="X band">X band</a>, 8–12 GHz. Extremely high gain ~70 dBi.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Parabolic_antenna" title="Parabolic antenna">Parabolic</a></i> - The most widely used high gain antenna at microwave frequencies and above. Consists of a dish-shaped metal <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_reflector" title="Parabolic reflector">parabolic reflector</a> with a feed antenna at the focus. It can have some of the highest gains of any antenna type, up to 60 dBi, but the dish must be large compared to a wavelength. Used for <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> antennas, point-to-point data links, satellite communication, and <a href="/wiki/Radio_telescope" title="Radio telescope">radio telescopes</a></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Schwarzbeck_BBHA_9120_D.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Schwarzbeck_BBHA_9120_D.jpg/150px-Schwarzbeck_BBHA_9120_D.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Schwarzbeck_BBHA_9120_D.jpg/225px-Schwarzbeck_BBHA_9120_D.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Schwarzbeck_BBHA_9120_D.jpg/300px-Schwarzbeck_BBHA_9120_D.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Schwarzbeck_BBHA_9120_D.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Microwave <a href="/wiki/Horn_antenna" title="Horn antenna">horn antenna</a> bandwidth 0.8–18 GHz</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Horn_antenna" title="Horn antenna">Horn</a></i> - Simple antenna with moderate gains of 15 to 25 dBi consists of a flaring metal horn attached to a <a href="/wiki/Waveguide" title="Waveguide">waveguide</a>. Used for applications such as <a href="/wiki/Radar_gun" title="Radar gun">radar guns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microwave_radiometer" title="Microwave radiometer">radiometers</a> and as <a href="/wiki/Antenna_feed" title="Antenna feed">feed antennas</a> for parabolic dishes.</li>
</ul>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar_antennas_on_USS_Theodore_Roosevelt_SPS-64.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="562" data-file-width="533" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Radar_antennas_on_USS_Theodore_Roosevelt_SPS-64.jpg/150px-Radar_antennas_on_USS_Theodore_Roosevelt_SPS-64.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Radar_antennas_on_USS_Theodore_Roosevelt_SPS-64.jpg/225px-Radar_antennas_on_USS_Theodore_Roosevelt_SPS-64.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Radar_antennas_on_USS_Theodore_Roosevelt_SPS-64.jpg/300px-Radar_antennas_on_USS_Theodore_Roosevelt_SPS-64.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar_antennas_on_USS_Theodore_Roosevelt_SPS-64.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/X_band" title="X band">X band</a> <a href="/wiki/Marine_radar" title="Marine radar">marine radar</a> <a href="/wiki/Slot_antenna" title="Slot antenna">slot antenna</a> on ship, 8 – 12 GHz.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Slot_antenna" title="Slot antenna">Slot</a></i> - Consist of a <a href="/wiki/Waveguide_(electromagnetism)" title="Waveguide (electromagnetism)">waveguide</a> with one or more slots cut in it to emit the microwaves. Linear slot antennas emit narrow fan-shaped beams. Used as UHF broadcast antennas and <a href="/wiki/Marine_radar" title="Marine radar">marine radar</a> antennas.</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Dielectric_resonator_antenna" title="Dielectric resonator antenna">Dielectric resonator</a></i> - consists of small ball or puck-shaped piece of <a href="/wiki/Dielectric" title="Dielectric">dielectric</a> material excited by aperture in waveguide Used at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millimeter_wave" title="Millimeter wave">millimeter wave</a> frequencies</li>
</ul>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traveling_wave">Traveling wave</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Traveling wave">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Unlike the above antennas, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traveling_wave_antenna" title="Traveling wave antenna">traveling wave antennas</a> are <a href="/wiki/Resonance" title="Resonance">nonresonant</a> so they have inherently broad bandwidth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aksoy_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-Aksoy-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> They are typically wire antennas multiple wavelengths long, through which the voltage and current waves travel in one direction, instead of bouncing back and forth to form <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave" title="Standing wave">standing waves</a> as in resonant antennas. They have <a href="/wiki/Linear_polarization" title="Linear polarization">linear polarization</a> (except for the helical antenna). Unidirectional traveling wave antennas are terminated by a resistor at one end equal to the antenna's <a href="/wiki/Characteristic_impedance" title="Characteristic impedance">characteristic resistance</a>, to absorb the waves from one direction. This makes them inefficient as transmitting antennas.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wire_inverted_L_antenna_construction.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="954" data-file-width="1676" height="85" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Wire_inverted_L_antenna_construction.png/150px-Wire_inverted_L_antenna_construction.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Wire_inverted_L_antenna_construction.png/225px-Wire_inverted_L_antenna_construction.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Wire_inverted_L_antenna_construction.png/300px-Wire_inverted_L_antenna_construction.png 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wire_inverted_L_antenna_construction.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A typical <a href="/wiki/Random_wire_antenna" title="Random wire antenna">random wire antenna</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shortwave" title="Shortwave">shortwave</a> reception, strung between two buildings.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Random_wire_antenna" title="Random wire antenna">Random wire</a></i> - This describes the typical antenna used to receive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shortwave" title="Shortwave">shortwave</a> radio, consisting of a random length of wire either strung outdoors between supports or indoors in a zigzag pattern along walls, connected to the receiver at one end. Can have complex radiation patterns with several lobes at angles to the wire.</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Beverage_antenna" title="Beverage antenna">Beverage</a></i> - Simplest unidirectional traveling wave antenna. Consists of a straight wire one to several wavelengths long, suspended near the ground, connected to the receiver at one end and terminated by a resistor equal to its <a href="/wiki/Characteristic_impedance" title="Characteristic impedance">characteristic impedance</a>, 400 to 800Ω at the other end. Its radiation pattern has a <a href="/wiki/Main_lobe" title="Main lobe">main lobe</a> at a shallow angle in the sky off the terminated end. It is used for reception of <a href="/wiki/Skywave" title="Skywave">skywaves</a> reflected off the ionosphere in long distance "skip" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shortwave" title="Shortwave">shortwave</a> communication.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moosbrunn_Quadrantantennen.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2140" data-file-width="2959" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Moosbrunn_Quadrantantennen.JPG/150px-Moosbrunn_Quadrantantennen.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Moosbrunn_Quadrantantennen.JPG/225px-Moosbrunn_Quadrantantennen.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Moosbrunn_Quadrantantennen.JPG/300px-Moosbrunn_Quadrantantennen.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moosbrunn_Quadrantantennen.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Quadrant antenna, similar to <a href="/wiki/Rhombic_antenna" title="Rhombic antenna">rhombic</a>, at an Austrian shortwave broadcast station. Radiates horizontal beam at 5-9 MHz, 100 kW</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Rhombic_antenna" title="Rhombic antenna">Rhombic</a></i> - Consists of four equal wire sections shaped like a <a href="/wiki/Rhombus" title="Rhombus">rhombus</a>. It is fed by a balanced feedline at one of the acute corners, and the two sides are connected to a resistor equal to the characteristic resistance of the antenna at the other. It has a main lobe in a horizontal direction off the terminated end of the rhombus. Used for <a href="/wiki/Skywave" title="Skywave">skywave</a> communication on shortwave bands.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Traqueur_acquisition.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="1728" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Traqueur_acquisition.JPG/150px-Traqueur_acquisition.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Traqueur_acquisition.JPG/225px-Traqueur_acquisition.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Traqueur_acquisition.JPG/300px-Traqueur_acquisition.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Traqueur_acquisition.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Array of four axial-mode <a href="/wiki/Helical_antenna" title="Helical antenna">helical antennas</a> used for satellite tracking, France</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Helical_antenna" title="Helical antenna">Helical (axial mode)</a></i> - Consists of a wire in the shape of a <a href="/wiki/Helix" title="Helix">helix</a> mounted above a reflecting screen. It radiates <a href="/wiki/Circular_polarization" title="Circular polarization">circularly polarized</a> waves in a beam off the end, with a typical gain of 15 dBi. It is used at VHF and UHF frequencies. Often used for satellite communication, which uses circular polarization because it is insensitive to the relative rotation on the beam axis.</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Leaky_wave_antenna" title="Leaky wave antenna">Leaky wave</a></i> - Microwave antennas consisting of a waveguide or coaxial cable with a slot or apertures cut in it so it radiates continuously along its length.</li>
</ul>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_of_ground">Effect of ground</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Effect of ground">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Multipath_propagation" title="Multipath propagation">Multipath propagation</a></div>
<p>Ground reflections is one of the common types of multipath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The radiation pattern and even the driving point impedance of an antenna can be influenced by the dielectric constant and especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">conductivity</a> of nearby objects. For a terrestrial antenna, the ground is usually one such object of importance. The antenna's height above the ground, as well as the electrical properties (<a href="/wiki/Permittivity" title="Permittivity">permittivity</a> and conductivity) of the ground, can then be important. Also, in the particular case of a monopole antenna, the ground (or an artificial <a href="/wiki/Ground_plane" title="Ground plane">ground plane</a>) serves as the return connection for the antenna current thus having an additional effect, particularly on the impedance seen by the feed line.</p>
<p>When an electromagnetic wave strikes a plane surface such as the ground, part of the wave is transmitted into the ground and part of it is reflected, according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fresnel_coefficients" title="Fresnel coefficients">Fresnel coefficients</a>. If the ground is a very good conductor then almost all of the wave is reflected (180° out of phase), whereas a ground modeled as a (lossy) dielectric can absorb a large amount of the wave's power. The power remaining in the reflected wave, and the phase shift upon reflection, strongly depend on the wave's <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_incidence_(optics)" title="Angle of incidence (optics)">angle of incidence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a>. The dielectric constant and conductivity (or simply the complex dielectric constant) is dependent on the soil type and is a function of frequency.</p>
<p>For <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VLF" title="VLF">very low frequencies</a> to <a href="/wiki/High_frequency" title="High frequency">high frequencies</a> (&lt;30 MHz), the ground behaves as a lossy <a href="/wiki/Dielectric" title="Dielectric">dielectric</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> thus the ground is characterized both by a <a href="/wiki/Electrical_resistivity_and_conductivity" title="Electrical resistivity and conductivity">conductivity</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Permittivity" title="Permittivity">permittivity</a> (dielectric constant) which can be measured for a given soil (but is influenced by fluctuating moisture levels) or can be estimated from certain maps. At lower frequencies the ground acts mainly as a good conductor, which AM middle wave broadcast (.5 - 1.6 MHz) antennas depend on.</p>
<p>At frequencies between 3 and 30 MHz, a large portion of the energy from a horizontally polarized antenna reflects off the ground, with almost total reflection at the grazing angles important for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_wave" title="Ground wave">ground wave</a> propagation. That reflected wave, with its phase reversed, can either cancel or reinforce the direct wave, depending on the antenna height in wavelengths and elevation angle (for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sky_wave" title="Sky wave">sky wave</a>).</p>
<p>On the other hand, vertically polarized radiation is not well reflected by the ground except at grazing incidence or over very highly conducting surfaces such as sea water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> However the grazing angle reflection important for ground wave propagation, using vertical polarization, is <i>in phase</i> with the direct wave, providing a boost of up to 6 db, as is detailed below.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:243px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A6-1EN.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="169" data-file-width="241" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/53/A6-1EN.jpg" width="241"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">The wave reflected by earth can be considered as emitted by the image antenna.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At VHF and above (&gt;30 MHz) the ground becomes a poorer reflector. However it remains a good reflector especially for horizontal polarization and grazing angles of incidence. That is important as these higher frequencies usually depend on horizontal <a href="/wiki/Line-of-sight_propagation" title="Line-of-sight propagation">line-of-sight propagation</a> (except for satellite communications), the ground then behaving almost as a mirror.</p>
<p>The net quality of a ground reflection depends on the topography of the surface. When the irregularities of the surface are much smaller than the wavelength, the dominant regime is that of <a href="/wiki/Specular_reflection" title="Specular reflection">specular reflection</a>, and the receiver sees both the real antenna and an image of the antenna under the ground due to reflection. But if the ground has irregularities not small compared to the wavelength, reflections will not be coherent but shifted by random phases. With shorter wavelengths (higher frequencies), this is generally the case.</p>
<p>Whenever both the receiving or transmitting antenna are placed at significant heights above the ground (relative to the wavelength), waves specularly reflected by the ground will travel a longer distance than direct waves, inducing a phase shift which can sometimes be significant. When a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sky_wave" title="Sky wave">sky wave</a> is launched by such an antenna, that phase shift is always significant unless the antenna is very close to the ground (compared to the wavelength).</p>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text">
