<div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(January 2014)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The phase of reflection of electromagnetic waves depends on the <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a> of the incident wave. Given the larger <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> of the ground (typically <i>n</i>=2) compared to air (<i>n</i>=1), the phase of horizontally polarized radiation is reversed upon reflection (a phase shift of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="1">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \scriptstyle {\pi }}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\scriptstyle {\pi }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8a04dac5e56b4dd97cf859469c415778ba0ec316" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.949ex; height:1.343ex;"/></span> radians or 180°). On the other hand, the vertical component of the wave's electric field is reflected at grazing angles of incidence approximately <i>in phase</i>. These phase shifts apply as well to a ground modelled as a good electrical conductor.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:250px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A6-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="145" data-file-width="248" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cd/A6-2.jpg" width="248"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">The currents in an antenna appear as an image in <i>opposite</i> phase when reflected at grazing angles. This causes a phase reversal for waves emitted by a horizontally polarized antenna (left) but not a vertically polarized antenna (center).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>This means that a receiving antenna "sees" an image of the antenna but with reversed currents. That current is in the same absolute direction as the actual antenna if the antenna is vertically oriented (and thus vertically polarized) but opposite the actual antenna if the antenna current is horizontal.</p>
<p>The actual antenna which is <i>transmitting</i> the original wave then also may <i>receive</i> a strong signal from its own image from the ground. This will induce an additional current in the antenna element, changing the current at the feedpoint for a given feedpoint voltage. Thus the antenna's impedance, given by the ratio of feedpoint voltage to current, is altered due to the antenna's proximity to the ground. This can be quite a significant effect when the antenna is within a wavelength or two of the ground. But as the antenna height is increased, the reduced power of the reflected wave (due to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inverse_square_law" title="Inverse square law">inverse square law</a>) allows the antenna to approach its asymptotic feedpoint impedance given by theory. At lower heights, the effect on the antenna's impedance is <i>very</i> sensitive to the exact distance from the ground, as this affects the phase of the reflected wave relative to the currents in the antenna. Changing the antenna's height by a quarter wavelength, then changes the phase of the reflection by 180°, with a completely different effect on the antenna's impedance.</p>
<p>The ground reflection has an important effect on the net far field <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pattern" title="Radiation pattern">radiation pattern</a> in the vertical plane, that is, as a function of elevation angle, which is thus different between a vertically and horizontally polarized antenna. Consider an antenna at a height <i>h</i> above the ground, transmitting a wave considered at the elevation angle θ. For a vertically polarized transmission the magnitude of the electric field of the electromagnetic wave produced by the direct ray plus the reflected ray is:</p>

<dd>
<dl>

<p>where:</p>

<li><i>θ</i> is the elevation angle of the wave being considered.</li>
<li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<div class="thumb tright">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\scriptstyle {\theta =0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6c8d219c577f1f2a333254e819dd4c7d38fc1ab0" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.894ex; height:1.843ex;"/></span>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For horizontal propagation between transmitting and receiving antennas situated near the ground reasonably far from each other, the distances traveled by the direct and reflected rays are nearly the same. There is almost no relative phase shift. If the emission is polarized vertically, the two fields (direct and reflected) add and there is maximum of received signal. If the signal is polarized horizontally, the two signals subtract and the received signal is largely cancelled. The vertical plane radiation patterns are shown in the image at right. With vertical polarization there is always a maximum for <i>θ</i>=0, horizontal propagation (left pattern). For horizontal polarization, there is cancellation at that angle. Note that the above formulae and these plots assume the ground as a perfect conductor. These plots of the radiation pattern correspond to a distance between the antenna and its image of 2.5λ. As the antenna height is increased, the number of lobes increases as well.</p>
<p>The difference in the above factors for the case of <i>θ</i>=0 is the reason that most broadcasting (transmissions intended for the public) uses vertical polarization. For receivers near the ground, horizontally polarized transmissions suffer cancellation. For best reception the receiving antennas for these signals are likewise vertically polarized. In some applications where the receiving antenna must work in any position, as in <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone" title="Mobile phone">mobile phones</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Base_station" title="Base station">base station</a> antennas use mixed polarization, such as linear polarization at an angle (with both vertical and horizontal components) or <a href="/wiki/Circular_polarization" title="Circular polarization">circular polarization</a>.</p>
<p>On the other hand, analog television transmissions are usually horizontally polarized, because in urban areas buildings can reflect the electromagnetic waves and create <a href="/wiki/Ghosting_(television)" title="Ghosting (television)">ghost images</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Multipath_propagation" title="Multipath propagation">multipath propagation</a>. Using horizontal polarization, ghosting is reduced because the amount of reflection in the horizontal polarization off the side of a building is generally less than in the vertical direction. Vertically polarized analog television have been used in some rural areas. In <a href="/wiki/Digital_terrestrial_television" title="Digital terrestrial television">digital terrestrial television</a> such reflections are less problematic, due to robustness of binary transmissions and <a href="/wiki/Error_detection_and_correction" title="Error detection and correction">error correction</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mutual_impedance_and_interaction_between_antennas">Mutual impedance and interaction between antennas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antenna_(radio)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Mutual impedance and interaction between antennas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Current circulating in one antenna generally induces a voltage across the feedpoint of nearby antennas or antenna elements. The mathematics presented below are useful in analyzing the electrical behaviour of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antenna_array_(electromagnetic)" title="Antenna array (electromagnetic)">antenna arrays</a>, where the properties of the individual array elements (such as half wave dipoles) are already known. If those elements were widely separated and driven in a certain amplitude and phase, then each would act independently as that element is known to. However, because of the mutual interaction between their electric and magnetic fields due to proximity, the currents in each element are <i>not</i> simply a function of the applied voltage (according to its driving point impedance), but depend on the currents in the other nearby elements. This now is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-field_region" title="Near-field region">near field</a> phenomenon which could not be properly accounted for using the <a href="/wiki/Friis_transmission_equation" title="Friis transmission equation">Friis transmission formula</a> for instance. This near field effect creates a different set of currents at the antenna terminals resulting in distortions in the far field radiation patterns; however, the distortions may be removed using a simple set of network equations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>The elements' feedpoint currents and voltages can be related to each other using the concept of <b>mutual impedance</b> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="1">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msub>
<mi>Z</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>j</mi>

<semantics>

</mstyle>

<li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>

</ul>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:368px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zij-en.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="356" data-file-width="366" height="356" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0d/Zij-en.png" width="366"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">Mutual impedance between parallel <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="1">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \scriptstyle {\lambda  \over 2}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\scriptstyle {\lambda  \over 2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/03c647f1023cef947013ed7d52f65251b67e9c6c" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:1.62ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span> dipoles not staggered. Curves <b>Re</b> and <b>Im</b> are the resistive and reactive parts of the impedance.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As is the case for mutual inductances,</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="1">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msub>
<mi>Z</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
<mi>j</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mo>=</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<msub>
<mi>Z</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>j</mi>
<mi>i</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \scriptstyle {Z_{ij}\,=\,Z_{ji}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\scriptstyle {Z_{{ij}}\,=\,Z_{{ji}}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/553bc6df8503139ca55179609f1f904beb9397b0" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:7.159ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>This is a consequence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorentz_reciprocity" title="Lorentz reciprocity">Lorentz reciprocity</a>. For an antenna element <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>i</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle i}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="i" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/add78d8608ad86e54951b8c8bd6c8d8416533d20" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.813ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> not connected to anything (open circuited) one can write <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>i</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle i_{i}=0}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="i_{i}=0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/abb660a5bf45979239b5b35638dbf881a26d96b1" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:5.902ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>. But for an element <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>i</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle i}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="i" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/add78d8608ad86e54951b8c8bd6c8d8416533d20" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.813ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> which is short circuited, a current is generated across that short but no voltage is allowed, so the corresponding <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
</mstyle>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \textstyle {v_{i}}=0}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\textstyle {v_{i}}=0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cbea14fb6dffb7a843a0697531ced809ddfd4dfd" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:6.227ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>. This is the case, for instance, with the so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasitic_element" title="Parasitic element">parasitic elements</a> of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yagi-Uda_antenna#Analysis" title="Yagi-Uda antenna">Yagi-Uda antenna</a> where the solid rod can be viewed as a dipole antenna shorted across its feedpoint. Parasitic elements are unpowered elements that absorb and reradiate RF energy according to the induced current calculated using such a system of equations.</p>
<p>With a particular geometry, it is possible for the mutual impedance between nearby antennas to be zero. This is the case, for instance, between the crossed dipoles used in the <a href="/wiki/Turnstile_antenna" title="Turnstile antenna">turnstile antenna</a>.</p>
